It\'s calculated as 19,940.14/(365,246.17+19,940.14).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li><li value="3">Two more TLDs have been assigned, but are not used: <a href="/wiki/.sj" title=".sj">.sj</a> for Svalbard and Jan Mayen; <a href="/wiki/.bv" title=".bv">.bv</a> for <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a>.</li>\n</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Norway</b> (<a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>: <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Norge_(country).ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/No-Norge.oga" class="internal" title="No-Norge.oga"><i>Norge</i></a></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokm\xc3\xa5l">Bokm\xc3\xa5l</a>) or <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:No-Noreg.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/No-Noreg.oga" class="internal" title="No-Noreg.oga"><i>Noreg</i></a></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a>); <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sami_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Sami language">Northern Sami</a>: <i lang="se">Norga</i>), officially the <b>Kingdom of Norway</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic country">Nordic country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Europe" title="Northwestern Europe">Northwestern Europe</a> whose core territory comprises the western and northernmost portion of the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Peninsula" title="Scandinavian Peninsula">Scandinavian Peninsula</a>; the remote island of <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> and the archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> are also part of the Kingdom of Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> The Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_Island" title="Peter I Island">Peter I Island</a> and the sub-Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a> are <a href="/wiki/Dependencies_of_Norway" title="Dependencies of Norway">dependent territories</a> and thus not considered part of the kingdom. Norway also lays claim to a section of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">Queen Maud Land</a>.\n</p><p>Norway has a total area of 385,252 square kilometres (148,747&#160;sq&#160;mi) and a population of 5,302,778 (as of April 2018).<sup id="cite_ref-Population_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The country shares a long eastern border with <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_land_borders" title="List of countries and territories by land borders">1,619 km</a> or 1,006&#160;mi long). Norway is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the north-east, and the <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a> strait to the south, with <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> on the other side. Norway has an extensive coastline, facing the North Atlantic Ocean and the <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/King_Harald_V" class="mw-redirect" title="King Harald V">King Harald V</a> of the Dano-German <a href="/wiki/House_of_Gl%C3%BCcksburg" title="House of Gl\xc3\xbccksburg">House of Gl\xc3\xbccksburg</a> is the current <a href="/wiki/King_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Norway">King of Norway</a>. <a href="/wiki/Erna_Solberg" title="Erna Solberg">Erna Solberg</a> became prime minister in 2013 and was reelected in September 2017. Erna Solberg replaced <a href="/wiki/Jens_Stoltenberg" title="Jens Stoltenberg">Jens Stoltenberg</a> who was the prime minister between 2000 and 2001 and 2005\xe2\x80\x932013. A <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, Norway <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Separation of power">divides state power</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabinet of Norway">cabinet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Norway" title="Supreme Court of Norway">Supreme Court</a>, as determined by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">1814 Constitution</a>. The kingdom was established as a merger of a large number of <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdoms_of_Norway" title="Petty kingdoms of Norway">petty kingdoms</a>. By the traditional count from <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hafrsfjord" title="Battle of Hafrsfjord">872</a>, the kingdom has existed continuously for 1,145 years, and the list of Norwegian monarchs includes over sixty kings and earls. From 1537 to 1814, Norway was a part of the Kingdom of Denmark, and from 1814 to 1905, it was in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with the Kingdom of Sweden.\n</p><p>Norway has both administrative and political subdivisions on two levels: <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Norway" title="Counties of Norway">counties</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_municipalities_of_Norway" title="List of municipalities of Norway">municipalities</a>. The <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1mi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="S\xc3\xa1mi people">S\xc3\xa1mi people</a> have a certain amount of self-determination and influence over traditional territories through the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliament_of_Norway" title="Sami Parliament of Norway">S\xc3\xa1mi Parliament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Finnmark_Act" title="Finnmark Act">Finnmark Act</a>. Norway <a href="/wiki/Norway_%E2%80%93_European_Union_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Norway \xe2\x80\x93 European Union relations">maintains close ties</a> with both the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States-Norway_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="United States-Norway relations">United States</a>. Norway is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic Treaty</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>; a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">WTO</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a>; and a part of the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a>.\n</p><p>Norway maintains the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_welfare_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Nordic welfare model">Nordic welfare model</a> with <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> and a comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Social_security" title="Social security">social security</a> system, and its values are rooted in egalitarian ideals.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The Norwegian state has large ownership positions in key industrial sectors, having extensive reserves of petroleum, natural gas, minerals, lumber, seafood, and fresh water. The <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">petroleum industry</a> accounts for around a quarter of the country\'s gross domestic product (GDP).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> On a <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per-capita</a> basis, Norway is the world\'s largest producer of oil and natural gas outside of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The country has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">fourth-highest</a> per capita income in the world on the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a> lists.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a>\'s GDP (PPP) per capita list (2015 estimate) which includes autonomous territories and regions, Norway ranks as number eleven.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> It has the world\'s largest <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_wealth_fund" title="Sovereign wealth fund">sovereign wealth fund</a>, with a value of USD 1&#160;trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Norway has had the highest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> ranking in the world since 2009, a position also held previously between 2001 and 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It also has the highest inequality-adjusted ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-HDI2_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI2-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Norway ranked first on the <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> for 2017<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and currently ranks first on the <a href="/wiki/OECD_Better_Life_Index" title="OECD Better Life Index">OECD Better Life Index</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Global_Integrity" title="Global Integrity">Index of Public Integrity</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Norway has one of the lowest crime rates in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Human_rights"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Human rights</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Cinema"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cinema</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Research"><span tocsection-43"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">7.8</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Sports"><span class="tocnumber">7.9</span> <span class="toctext">Sports</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#International_rankings"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">International rankings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ohthere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ohthere.jpg/330px-Ohthere.jpg" width="330" height="78" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ohthere.jpg/495px-Ohthere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Ohthere.jpg/660px-Ohthere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="677" data-file-height="161" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ohthere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Opening of <a href="/wiki/Ohthere_of_H%C3%A5logaland" title="Ohthere of H\xc3\xa5logaland">Ohthere\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> account, translated: "Ohthere told his lord <i>\xc3\x86lfrede</i> king that he lived northmost of all <span class="nowrap">Norwegians\xe2\x80\xa6"</span></div></div></div>\n<p>Norway has two official names: <i>Norge</i> in <a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokm\xc3\xa5l">Bokm\xc3\xa5l</a> and <i>Noreg</i> in <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a>. The English name Norway comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>Nor\xc3\xbeweg</i> mentioned in 880, meaning "northern way" or "way leading to the north", which is how the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxons</a> referred to the coastline of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> Norway<sup id="cite_ref-Nomino-NRK_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nomino-NRK-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tvil-Forskning_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvil-Forskning-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> similar to scientific consensus about the origin of the Norwegian language name.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxons of Britain also referred to the kingdom of Norway in 880 as <i>Nor\xc3\xb0manna land</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nomino-NRK_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nomino-NRK-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tvil-Forskning_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvil-Forskning-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is some disagreement about whether the native name of Norway originally had the same etymology as the English form. According to the traditional dominant view, the first component was originally <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nor%C3%B0r" class="extiw" title="wikt:nor\xc3\xb0r"><i>nor\xc3\xb0r</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> of English <i>north</i>, so the full name was <i>Nor\xc3\xb0r <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vegr" class="extiw" title="wikt:vegr">vegr</a></i>, "the way northwards", referring to the sailing route along the Norwegian coast, and contrasting with <i>su\xc3\xb0rvegar</i> "southern way" (from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/su%C3%B0r" class="extiw" title="wikt:su\xc3\xb0r">su\xc3\xb0r</a>) for (Germany), and <i>austrvegr</i> "eastern way" (from <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/austr" class="extiw" title="wikt:austr">austr</a>) for the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a>. In the translation of <a href="/wiki/Orosius" title="Orosius">Orosius</a> for Alfred, the name is <i>Nor\xc3\xb0weg</i>, while in younger Old English sources the \xc3\xb0 is gone.<sup id="cite_ref-Eldar_Heide_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eldar_Heide-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In the 10th century many norsemen settled in Northern France, according to the sagas, in the area that was later called <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> from <i>nor\xc3\xb0mann</i> (norseman or Scandinavian<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>), although not a Norwegian possession.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In France <i>normanni</i> or <i>northmanni</i> referred to people of Norway, Sweden or Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Until around 1800 inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Western_Norway" title="Western Norway">Western Norway</a> where referred to as <i>nordmenn</i> (northmen) while inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Norway" title="Eastern Norway">Eastern Norway</a> where referred to as <i>austmenn</i> (eastmen).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to another theory, the first component was a word <i>n\xc3\xb3r</i>, meaning "narrow" (Old English <i>nearu</i>) or "northern", referring to the inner-<a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> sailing route through the land ("narrow way"). The interpretation as "northern", as reflected in the English and Latin forms of the name, would then have been due to later <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a>. This latter view originated with philologist Niels Halvorsen Tr\xc3\xb8nnes in 1847; since 2016 it as also advocated by language student and activist Klaus Johan Myrvoll and was adopted by <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a> professor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Schulte_(professor)" title="Michael Schulte (professor)">Michael Schulte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nomino-NRK_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nomino-NRK-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tvil-Forskning_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvil-Forskning-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The form <i>Nore</i> is still used in placenames such as the village of <a href="/wiki/Nore,_Norway" title="Nore, Norway">Nore</a> and lake <a href="/wiki/Norefjorden" title="Norefjorden">Norefjorden</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buskerud" title="Buskerud">Buskerud</a> county, and still has the same meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-Nomino-NRK_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nomino-NRK-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tvil-Forskning_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvil-Forskning-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Among other arguments in favour of the theory, it is pointed out that the word has a long vowel in Skaldic poetry and is not attested with &lt;\xc3\xb0&gt; in any native Norse texts or inscriptions (the earliest runic attestations have the spellings <i>nuruiak</i> and <i>nuriki</i>). This resurrected theory has received some pushback by other scholars on various grounds, e.&#160;g. the uncontroversial presence of the element <i>nor\xc3\xb0r</i> in the ethnonym <i>nor\xc3\xb0rma\xc3\xb0r</i> "Norseman, Norwegian person" (modern Norwegian <i>nordmann</i>), and the adjective <i>norr\xc7\xbfnn</i> "northern, Norse, Norwegian", as well as the very early attestations of the Latin and Anglo-Saxon forms with &lt;th&gt;.<sup id="cite_ref-Eldar_Heide_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eldar_Heide-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tvil-Forskning_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvil-Forskning-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a Latin manuscript of 849, the name <i>Northuagia</i> is mentioned, while a French chronicle of c. 900 uses the names <i>Northwegia</i> and <i>Norwegia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sigurdsson_Riisoy_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigurdsson_Riisoy-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Ohthere_of_H%C3%A5logaland" title="Ohthere of H\xc3\xa5logaland">Ohthere of H\xc3\xa5logaland</a> visited King <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> in England in the end of the ninth century, the land was called <i>Nor\xc3\xb0wegr</i> (lit. "Northway") and <i>nor\xc3\xb0manna land</i> (lit. "Northmen\'s land").<sup id="cite_ref-Sigurdsson_Riisoy_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigurdsson_Riisoy-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> According to Ohthere, <i>Nor\xc3\xb0manna</i> lived along the Atlantic coast, the Danes around Skagerrak og Kattegat, while the Sami people (the "Fins") had a nomadic lifestyle in the wide interior.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Ohthere told Alfred that he was "the most northern of all Norwegians", presumably at <a href="/wiki/Senja" title="Senja">Senja</a> island or closer to <a href="/wiki/Troms%C3%B8" title="Troms\xc3\xb8">Troms\xc3\xb8</a>. He also said that beyond the wide wilderness in Norway\'s southern part was the land of the Swedes, "Svealand".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The adjective <i>Norwegian</i>, recorded from c. 1600, is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Latin" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Latin">latinisation</a> of the name as <i>Norwegia</i>; in the adjective <i>Norwegian</i>, the Old English spelling \'-weg\' has survived.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Norway had become Christian, <i>Noregr</i> and <i>Noregi</i> had become the most common forms, but during the 15th century, the newer forms <i>Noreg(h)</i> and <i>Norg(h)e</i>, found in medieval Icelandic manuscripts, took over and have survived until the modern day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Norway" title="History of Norway">History of Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scandinavia" title="History of Scandinavia">History of Scandinavia</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_prehistory" title="Scandinavian prehistory">Scandinavian prehistory</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_boats.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Bronze_Age_boats.png/220px-Bronze_Age_boats.png" width="220" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Bronze_Age_boats.png/330px-Bronze_Age_boats.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Bronze_Age_boats.png/440px-Bronze_Age_boats.png 2x" data-file-width="3054" data-file-height="3336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_boats.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Rock_carvings" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock carvings">rock carvings</a> at <a href="/wiki/Steinkjer" title="Steinkjer">Steinkjer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Norway" title="Central Norway">Central Norway</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first inhabitants were the <a href="/wiki/Ahrensburg_culture" title="Ahrensburg culture">Ahrensburg culture</a> (11th to 10th millennia BC), which was a late <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> culture during the Younger Dryas, the last period of cold at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Weichselian_glaciation" title="Weichselian glaciation">Weichselian glaciation</a>. The culture is named after the village of <a href="/wiki/Ahrensburg" title="Ahrensburg">Ahrensburg</a>, 25&#160;km (15.53&#160;mi) north-east of <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">German state of Schleswig-Holstein</a>, where wooden arrow shafts and clubs have been excavated.<sup id="cite_ref-Genetics_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The earliest traces of human occupation in Norway are found along the coast, where the huge ice shelf of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last ice age</a> first melted between 11,000 and 8,000 BC. The oldest finds are stone tools dating from 9,500 to 6,000 BC, discovered in <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a> (<a href="/wiki/Komsa_culture" title="Komsa culture">Komsa culture</a>) in the north and <a href="/wiki/Rogaland" title="Rogaland">Rogaland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fosna-Hensbacka_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Fosna-Hensbacka culture">Fosna culture</a>) in the south-west. However, theories about two altogether different cultures (the Komsa culture north of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> being one and the Fosna culture from <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Tr\xc3\xb8ndelag">Tr\xc3\xb8ndelag</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oslofjord" title="Oslofjord">Oslofjord</a> being the other) were rendered obsolete in the 1970s.\n</p><p>More recent finds along the entire coast revealed to archaeologists that the difference between the two can simply be ascribed to different types of tools and not to different cultures. Coastal fauna provided a means of livelihood for fishermen and hunters, who may have made their way along the southern coast about 10,000 BC when the interior was still covered with ice. It is now thought that these so-called "Arctic" peoples came from the south and followed the coast northward considerably later.\n</p><p>In the southern part of the country are dwelling sites dating from about 5,000 BC. Finds from these sites give a clearer idea of the life of the hunting and fishing peoples. The implements vary in shape and mostly are made of different kinds of stone; those of later periods are more skilfully made. <a href="/wiki/Rock_carvings" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock carvings">Rock carvings</a> (i.e. petroglyphs) have been found, usually near hunting and fishing grounds. They represent game such as <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">elk</a>, bears, birds, <a href="/wiki/Earless_seal" title="Earless seal">seals</a>, whales, and fish (especially <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halibut" title="Halibut">halibut</a>), all of which were vital to the way of life of the coastal peoples. The <a href="/wiki/Rock_carvings_at_Alta" title="Rock carvings at Alta">rock carvings at Alta</a> in Finnmark, the largest in Scandinavia, were made at sea level from 4,200 to 500 BC and mark the progression of the land as the sea rose after the last ice age ended.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordanes,_Norway_tribes.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jordanes%2C_Norway_tribes.png/260px-Jordanes%2C_Norway_tribes.png" width="260" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jordanes%2C_Norway_tribes.png/390px-Jordanes%2C_Norway_tribes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Jordanes%2C_Norway_tribes.png 2x" data-file-width="456" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordanes,_Norway_tribes.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Locations of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes described by <a href="/wiki/Jordanes" title="Jordanes">Jordanes</a> in Norway</div></div></div>\n<p>Between 3000 and 2500 BC, new settlers (<a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">Corded Ware culture</a>) arrived in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Norway" title="Eastern Norway">eastern Norway</a>. They were <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Farmers" class="mw-redirect" title="Farmers">farmers</a> who grew grain and kept cows and sheep. The hunting-fishing population of the west coast was also gradually replaced by farmers, though hunting and fishing remained useful secondary means of livelihood.\n</p><p>From about 1500 BC, <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> was gradually introduced, but the use of stone implements continued; Norway had few riches to barter for bronze goods, and the few finds consist mostly of elaborate weapons and brooches that only chieftains could afford. Huge burial cairns built close to the sea as far north as <a href="/wiki/Harstad" title="Harstad">Harstad</a> and also inland in the south are characteristic of this period. The motifs of the rock carvings differ from those typical of the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. Representations of the Sun, animals, trees, weapons, ships, and people are all strongly stylised.\n</p><p>Thousands of <a href="/wiki/Rock_carving" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock carving">rock carvings</a> from this period depict ships, and the large stone burial monuments known as <a href="/wiki/Stone_ship" title="Stone ship">stone ships</a>, suggest that ships and seafaring played an important role in the culture at large. The depicted ships most likely represent sewn plank built canoes used for warfare, fishing and trade. These ship types may have their origin as far back as the neolithic period and they continue into the Pre-Roman Iron Age, as exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Hjortspring_boat" title="Hjortspring boat">Hjortspring boat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Iron Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Scandinavia" title="Iron Age Scandinavia">Iron Age Scandinavia</a></div>\n<p>Little has been found dating from the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> (the last 500 years BC). The dead were cremated, and their graves contain few burial goods. During the first four centuries AD, the people of Norway were in contact with Roman-occupied <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>. About 70 Roman bronze cauldrons, often used as burial urns, have been found. Contact with the civilised countries farther south brought a knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Runes" title="Runes">runes</a>; the oldest known Norwegian runic inscription dates from the 3rd century. At this time, the amount of settled area in the country increased, a development that can be traced by coordinated studies of <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, and place-names. The oldest root names, such as nes, vik, and b\xc3\xb8 ("cape," "bay," and "farm"), are of great antiquity, dating perhaps from the Bronze Age, whereas the earliest of the groups of compound names with the suffixes vin ("meadow") or heim ("settlement"), as in Bj\xc7\xabrgvin (Bergen) or S\xc7\xbfheim (Seim), usually date from the 1st century AD.\n</p><p>Archaeologists first made the decision to divide the Iron Age of Northern Europe into distinct pre-Roman and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Iron Age">Roman Iron Ages</a> after Emil Vedel unearthed a number of Iron Age artefacts in 1866 on the island of <a href="/wiki/Bornholm" title="Bornholm">Bornholm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> They did not exhibit the same permeating Roman influence seen in most other artefacts from the early centuries AD, indicating that parts of northern Europe had not yet come into contact with the Romans at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_period">Migration period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Migration period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Migration_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Migration period">Migration period</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdoms_of_Norway" title="Petty kingdoms of Norway">Petty kingdoms of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg/170px-Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg/255px-Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg/340px-Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1838" data-file-height="2646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_swords_at_Bergen_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Viking_sword" title="Viking sword">Viking swords</a> found in Norway, preserved at <a href="/wiki/Bergen_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Bergen Museum">Bergen Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> in the 5th century is characterised by rich finds, including <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">tribal chiefs</a>\' graves containing magnificent weapons and gold objects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Hill forts were built on precipitous rocks for defence. Excavation has revealed stone foundations of farmhouses 18 to 27 metres (59 to 89&#160;ft) long\xe2\x80\x94one even 46 metres (151 feet) long\xe2\x80\x94the roofs of which were supported on wooden posts. These houses were family homesteads where several generations lived together, with people and cattle under one roof.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>These states were based on either clans or tribes (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Horder" title="Horder">Horder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hordaland" title="Hordaland">Hordaland</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Norway" title="Western Norway">western Norway</a>). By the 9th century, each of these small states had <a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)"><i>things</i></a> (local or regional assemblies) for negotiating and settling disputes. The <i>thing</i> meeting places, each eventually with a <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B6rgr" title="H\xc3\xb6rgr">h\xc3\xb6rgr</a> (open-air sanctuary) or a <a href="/wiki/Heathen_hof" class="mw-redirect" title="Heathen hof">heathen hof</a> (temple; literally "hill"), were usually situated on the oldest and best farms, which belonged to the chieftains and wealthiest farmers. The regional <i>things</i> united to form even larger units: assemblies of deputy yeomen from several regions. In this way, the <i>lagting</i> (assemblies for negotiations and lawmaking) developed. The Gulating had its meeting place by <a href="/wiki/Sognefjord" title="Sognefjord">Sognefjord</a> and may have been the centre of an aristocratic confederation<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> along the western fjords and islands called the Gulatingslag. The Frostating was the assembly for the leaders in the <a href="/wiki/Trondheimsfjord" class="mw-redirect" title="Trondheimsfjord">Trondheimsfjord</a> area; the <a href="/wiki/Earls_of_Lade" title="Earls of Lade">Earls of Lade</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>, seem to have enlarged the Frostatingslag by adding the coastland from <a href="/wiki/Romsdalsfjord" title="Romsdalsfjord">Romsdalsfjord</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lofoten" title="Lofoten">Lofoten</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viking_Age">Viking Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Viking Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Norway" title="Unification of Norway">Unification of Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_Kingdom_of_Norway" title="Hereditary Kingdom of Norway">Hereditary Kingdom of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_(4835828216).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg/170px-Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg/255px-Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg/340px-Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_%284835828216%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_bateau_viking_dOseberg_(4835828216).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Oseberg_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Oseberg ship">Oseberg ship</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Ship_Museum_(Oslo)" title="Viking Ship Museum (Oslo)">Viking Ship Museum</a> in Oslo, Norway</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/150px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" width="150" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/225px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/300px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Gjermundbu_helmet" title="Gjermundbu helmet">Gjermundbu helmet</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Buskerud" title="Buskerud">Buskerud</a> is the only known reconstructable <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a> helmet</div></div></div>\n<p>From the 8th to the 10th century, the wider Scandinavian region was the source of <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Vikings</a>. The looting of the monastery at <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a> in Northeast England in 793 by <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse people</a> has long been regarded as the event which marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This age was characterised by expansion and emigration by Viking <a href="/wiki/Seafarer" class="mw-redirect" title="Seafarer">seafarers</a>. They <a href="/wiki/Colonise" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonise">colonised</a>, raided, and traded in all parts of Europe. Norwegian Viking explorers first discovered <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> by accident in the 9th century when heading for the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, and eventually came across <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a>, known today as <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, in Canada. The Vikings from Norway were most active in the northern and western <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> and eastern North America isles.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to tradition, <a href="/wiki/Harald_Fairhair" title="Harald Fairhair">Harald Fairhair</a> unified them into one in 872 after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hafrsfjord" title="Battle of Hafrsfjord">Battle of Hafrsfjord</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stavanger" title="Stavanger">Stavanger</a>, thus becoming the first king of a united Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Harald\'s realm was mainly a South Norwegian coastal state. Fairhair ruled with a strong hand and according to the sagas, many Norwegians left the country to live in Iceland, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> and Ireland. The modern-day Irish cities of <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limerick" title="Limerick">Limerick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a> were founded by Norwegian settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Z%C3%BCge,_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png/260px-Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png" width="260" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png/390px-Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png/520px-Z%C3%BCge%2C_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png 2x" data-file-width="4026" data-file-height="2502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Z%C3%BCge,_Landnahmen_und_Siedlungsgebiete_der_Nordm%C3%A4nner_-_800-1050.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Norwegian, Danish and Swedish expansion during the Viking age between 800\xe2\x80\x931050</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse traditions</a> were slowly replaced by <a href="/wiki/Christian_mythology" title="Christian mythology">Christian ones</a> in the late 10th and early 11th centuries. One of the most important sources for the history of the 11th century Vikings is the treaty between the Icelanders and Olaf Haraldsson, king of Norway circa 1015 to 1028.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This is largely attributed to the missionary kings <a href="/wiki/Olaf_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Olaf I of Norway">Olav Tryggvasson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Norway" title="Olaf II of Norway">St. Olav</a>. <a href="/wiki/Haakon_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Haakon I of Norway">Haakon the Good</a> was Norway\'s first Christian king, in the mid-10th century, though his attempt to introduce the religion was rejected. Born sometime in between 963\xe2\x80\x93969, Olav Tryggvasson set off raiding in England with 390 ships. He attacked London during this raiding. Arriving back in Norway in 995, Olav landed in <a href="/wiki/Moster_(island)" title="Moster (island)">Moster</a>. There he built a church which became the first <a href="/wiki/Old_Moster_Church" title="Old Moster Church">Christian church</a> ever built in Norway. From Moster, Olav sailed north to <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a> where he was proclaimed King of Norway by the Eyrathing in 995.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">Feudalism</a> never really developed in Norway or Sweden, as it did in the rest of Europe. However, the administration of government took on a very conservative feudal character. The <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> forced the royalty to cede to them greater and greater concessions over foreign trade and the economy. The League had this hold over the royalty because of the loans the Hansa had made to the royalty and the large debt the kings were carrying. The League\'s monopolistic control over the economy of Norway put pressure on all classes, especially the peasantry, to the degree that no real <a href="/wiki/Burgher_(title)" title="Burgher (title)">burgher</a> class existed in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_Norway_at_its_greatest_extent">Civil war and Norway at its greatest extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Civil war and Norway at its greatest extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Norway_(872%E2%80%931397)" title="Kingdom of Norway (872\xe2\x80\x931397)">Kingdom of Norway (872\xe2\x80\x931397)</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greater_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Norway">Greater Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_era_in_Norway" title="Civil war era in Norway">Civil war era in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png/220px-Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png/330px-Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png/440px-Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Norwegian_Hereditary_Empire_excluding_Greenland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Norway_(872%E2%80%931397)" title="Kingdom of Norway (872\xe2\x80\x931397)">Norwegian Kingdom</a> at its greatest extent, 13th century</div></div></div>\n<p>From the 1040s to 1130, the country was at peace.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  In 1130, the <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_era_in_Norway" title="Civil war era in Norway">civil war era</a> broke out on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Line_of_succession_to_the_Norwegian_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="Line of succession to the Norwegian throne">unclear succession laws</a>, which allowed all the king\'s sons to rule jointly. For periods there could be peace, before a lesser son allied himself with a chieftain and started a new conflict. The <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Nidaros" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Nidaros">Archdiocese of Nidaros</a> was created in 1152 and attempted to control the appointment of kings.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The church inevitably had to take sides in the conflicts, with the civil wars also becoming an issue regarding the church\'s influence of the king. The wars ended in 1217 with the appointment of <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A5kon_H%C3%A5konsson" class="mw-redirect" title="H\xc3\xa5kon H\xc3\xa5konsson">H\xc3\xa5kon H\xc3\xa5konsson</a>, who introduced clear law of succession.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1000 to 1300, the population increased from 150,000 to 400,000, resulting both in more land being cleared and the subdivision of farms. While in the Viking Age all farmers owned their own land, by 1300, seventy percent of the land was owned by the king, the church, or the aristocracy. This was a gradual process which took place because of farmers borrowing money in poor times and not being able to repay. However, tenants always remained free men and the large distances and often scattered ownership meant that they enjoyed much more freedom than continental serfs. In the 13th century, about twenty percent of a farmer\'s yield went to the king, church and landowners.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 14th century is described as Norway\'s <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a>, with peace and increase in trade, especially with the British Islands, although Germany became increasingly important towards the end of the century. Throughout the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, the king established Norway as a sovereign state with a central administration and local representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1349, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> spread to Norway and had within a year killed a third of the population. Later plagues reduced the population to half the starting point by 1400. Many communities were entirely wiped out, resulting in an abundance of land, allowing farmers to switch to more <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>. The reduction in taxes weakened the king\'s position,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and many aristocrats lost the basis for their surplus, reducing some to mere farmers. High <a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">tithes</a> to church made it increasingly powerful and the archbishop became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Council_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Council of State">Council of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s45_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s45-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bryggen,_Bergen3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/220px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/330px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/440px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2544" data-file-height="1696" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bryggen,_Bergen3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bryggen" title="Bryggen">Bryggen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>, once the center of trade in Norway under the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> trade network, now preserved as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> took control over Norwegian trade during the 14th century and established a trading center in <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>. In 1380, <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Denmark" title="Olaf II of Denmark">Olaf Haakonsson</a> inherited both the Norwegian and Danish thrones, creating a union between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-s45_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s45-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1397, under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_I_of_Denmark" title="Margaret I of Denmark">Margaret I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> was created between the three Scandinavian countries. She waged war against the Germans, resulting in a trade blockade and higher taxation on Norwegians, which resulted in <a href="/wiki/Engelbrekt_rebellion" title="Engelbrekt rebellion">a rebellion</a>. However, the Norwegian Council of State was too weak to pull out of the union.<sup id="cite_ref-s46_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s46-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Margaret pursued a centralising policy which inevitably favoured Denmark, because it had a greater population than Norway and Sweden combined.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Margaret also granted trade privileges to the Hanseatic merchants of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="L\xc3\xbcbeck">L\xc3\xbcbeck</a> in Bergen in return for recognition of her right to rule, and these hurt the Norwegian economy. The Hanseatic merchants formed a state within a state in Bergen for generations.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Even worse were the pirates, the "<a href="/wiki/Victual_Brothers" title="Victual Brothers">Victual Brothers</a>", who launched three devastating raids on the port (the last in 1427).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway slipped ever more to the background under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Oldenburg" title="House of Oldenburg">Oldenburg dynasty</a> (established 1448). There was one revolt under <a href="/wiki/Knut_Alvsson" title="Knut Alvsson">Knut Alvsson</a> in 1502.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Norwegians had some affection for <a href="/wiki/Christian_II_of_Denmark" title="Christian II of Denmark">King Christian II</a>, who resided in the country for several years. Norway took no part in the events which led to Swedish independence from Denmark in the 1520s.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kalmar_Union">Kalmar Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kalmar Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a></div>\n<p>Upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Haakon_V_of_Norway" title="Haakon V of Norway">Haakon V</a> (King of Norway) in 1319, <a href="/wiki/Magnus_IV_of_Sweden" title="Magnus IV of Sweden">Magnus Erikson</a>, at just three years old, inherited the throne as King Magnus VII of Norway. At the same time, a movement to make Magnus King of Sweden proved successful, and both the kings of Sweden and of Denmark were elected to the throne by their respective nobles, Thus, with his election to the throne of Sweden, both Sweden and Norway were united under King Magnus VII.<sup id="cite_ref-larsen_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larsen-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1349, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> radically altered Norway, killing between 50% and 60% of its population<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and leaving it in a period of social and economic decline.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The plague left Norway very poor. Although the death rate was comparable with the rest of Europe, economic recovery took much longer because of the small, scattered population.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Even before the plague, the population was only about 500,000.<sup id="cite_ref-end_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-end-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> After the plague, many farms lay idle while the population slowly increased.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> However, the few surviving farms\' tenants found their bargaining positions with their landlords greatly strengthened.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM,_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg/220px-Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg/330px-Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg/440px-Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM%2C_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="5500" data-file-height="4653" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlas_Van_der_Hagen-KW1049B10_007-NORVEGIA_REGNUM,_Vulgo_NOR-RYKE.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old map of Norway c. 1660 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>King Magnus VII ruled Norway until 1350, when his son, Haakon, was placed on the throne as <a href="/wiki/Haakon_VI_of_Norway" title="Haakon VI of Norway">Haakon VI</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1363, Haakon VI married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_I_of_Denmark" title="Margaret I of Denmark">Margaret</a>, the daughter of King <a href="/wiki/Valdemar_IV_of_Denmark" title="Valdemar IV of Denmark">Valdemar IV of Denmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Upon the death of Haakon VI, in 1379, his son, <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Denmark" title="Olaf II of Denmark">Olaf IV</a>, was only 10 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Olaf had already been elected to the throne of Denmark on 3 May 1376.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Thus, upon Olaf\'s accession to the throne of Norway, Denmark and Norway entered <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Olaf\'s mother and Haakon\'s widow, Queen Margaret, managed the foreign affairs of Denmark and Norway during the minority of Olaf IV.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Margaret was working toward a union of Sweden with Denmark and Norway by having Olaf elected to the Swedish throne. She was on the verge of achieving this goal when Olaf IV suddenly died.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> However, Denmark made Margaret temporary ruler upon the death of Olaf. On 2 February 1388, Norway followed suit and crowned Margaret.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_68-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Queen Margaret knew that her power would be more secure if she were able to find a king to rule in her place. She settled on <a href="/wiki/Eric_of_Pomerania" title="Eric of Pomerania">Eric of Pomerania</a>, grandson of her sister. Thus at an all-Scandinavian meeting held at Kalmar, Erik of Pomerania was crowned king of all three Scandinavian countries. Thus, royal politics resulted in personal unions between the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>, eventually bringing the thrones of Norway, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> under the control of Queen Margaret when the country entered into the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Denmark">Union with Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Union with Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Denmark%E2%80%93Norway" title="Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway">Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway</a></div>\n<p>After Sweden broke out of the <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a> in 1521, Norway tried to follow suit,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but the subsequent rebellion was defeated, and Norway remained in a union with Denmark until 1814, a total of 434 years. During the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">national romanticism</a> of the 19th century, this period was <a href="/wiki/Nicolai_Wergeland" title="Nicolai Wergeland">by some</a> referred to as the "400-Year Night", since all of the kingdom\'s royal, intellectual, and administrative power was centred in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> in Denmark. In fact, it was a period of great prosperity and progress for Norway, especially in terms of shipping and foreign trade, and it also secured the country\'s revival from the demographic catastrophe it suffered in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>. Based on the respective natural resources, Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway was in fact a very good match since Denmark supported Norway\'s needs for grain and food supplies, and Norway supplied Denmark with timber, metal, and fish.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_(Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten,_1660).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/220px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/330px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/440px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="647" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_(Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten,_1660).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Sound" title="Battle of the Sound">Battle of the Sound</a> between an allied Dano-Norwegian\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> fleet and the Swedish navy, 8 November 1658 (29 October <a href="/wiki/Old_Style" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Style">OS</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Denmark%E2%80%93Norway_and_Holstein" title="Reformation in Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway and Holstein">introduction of Protestantism</a> in 1536, the archbishopric in Trondheim was dissolved, and Norway lost its independence, and effectually became a colony of Denmark. The Church\'s incomes and possessions were instead redirected to the court in Copenhagen. Norway lost the steady stream of pilgrims to the relics of <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Norway" title="Olaf II of Norway">St. Olav</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Nidaros" title="Nidaros">Nidaros</a> shrine, and with them, much of the contact with cultural and economic life in the rest of Europe.\n</p><p>Eventually restored as a kingdom (albeit in legislative union with Denmark) in 1661, Norway saw its land area decrease in the 17th century with the loss of the provinces <a href="/wiki/Bohusl%C3%A4n" title="Bohusl\xc3\xa4n">B\xc3\xa5huslen</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A4mtland" title="J\xc3\xa4mtland">Jemtland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4rjedalen" title="H\xc3\xa4rjedalen">Herjedalen</a> to Sweden, as the result of a number of disastrous wars with Sweden. In the north, however, its territory was increased by the acquisition of the northern provinces of <a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a>, at the expense of Sweden and Russia.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1695%E2%80%931697" title="Great Famine of 1695\xe2\x80\x931697">famine of 1695\xe2\x80\x931696</a>  killed roughly 10% of Norway\'s population.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The harvest failed in Scandinavia at least nine times between 1740 and 1800, with great loss of life.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Sweden">Union with Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Union with Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdoms_of_Sweden_and_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdoms of Sweden and Norway">United Kingdoms of Sweden and Norway</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Norway_in_1814" class="mw-redirect" title="Norway in 1814">Norwegian protectorate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_romantic_nationalism" title="Norwegian romantic nationalism">Norwegian romantic nationalism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg/220px-Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg/330px-Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg/440px-Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3330" data-file-height="2234" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eidsvoll_riksraad_1814.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1814 constitutional assembly, painted by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wergeland" title="Oscar Wergeland">Oscar Wergeland</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway was attacked by the United Kingdom at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Copenhagen_(1807)" title="Battle of Copenhagen (1807)">Battle of Copenhagen</a>, it entered into an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, with the war leading to dire conditions and mass <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> in 1812. As the Danish kingdom found itself on the losing side in 1814, it was forced, under terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kiel" title="Treaty of Kiel">Treaty of Kiel</a>, to cede Norway to the king of Sweden, while the old Norwegian provinces of Iceland, Greenland, and the Faroe Islands remained with the Danish crown.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Norway took this opportunity to declare independence, adopted a constitution based on <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_France" title="Constitution of France">French</a> models, and elected the Crown Prince of Denmark and Norway, <a href="/wiki/Christian_VIII_of_Denmark" title="Christian VIII of Denmark">Christian Frederick</a>, as king on 17 May 1814. This is the famous <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Constitution_Day" title="Norwegian Constitution Day">Syttende Mai</a> (Seventeenth of May) holiday celebrated by Norwegians and Norwegian-Americans alike. <i>Syttende Mai</i> is also called <i>Norwegian Constitution Day</i>.\n</p><p>Norwegian opposition to the great powers\' decision to link Norway with Sweden caused the <a href="/wiki/Swedish%E2%80%93Norwegian_War_(1814)" title="Swedish\xe2\x80\x93Norwegian War (1814)">Norwegian\xe2\x80\x93Swedish War</a> to break out as Sweden tried to subdue Norway by military means. As Sweden\'s military was not strong enough to defeat the Norwegian forces outright, and Norway\'s treasury was not large enough to support a protracted war, and as British and Russian navies blockaded the Norwegian coast,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> the belligerents were forced to negotiate the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Moss" title="Convention of Moss">Convention of Moss</a>. According to the terms of the convention, Christian Frederik abdicated the Norwegian throne and authorised the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Norway">Parliament of Norway</a> to make the necessary constitutional amendments to allow for the <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> that Norway was forced to accept. On 4 November 1814, the Parliament (Storting) elected <a href="/wiki/Charles_XIII_of_Sweden" title="Charles XIII of Sweden">Charles XIII of Sweden</a> as king of Norway, thereby establishing the <a href="/wiki/Union_between_Sweden_and_Norway" title="Union between Sweden and Norway">union with Sweden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Under this arrangement, Norway kept its liberal constitution and its own independent institutions, except for the foreign service. Following the recession caused by the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, economic development of Norway remained slow until economic growth began around 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg/220px-Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg/330px-Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg/440px-Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Havreskjering_Fossheim_Lindahl.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harvesting of oats in <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8lster" title="J\xc3\xb8lster">J\xc3\xb8lster</a>, c. 1890</div></div></div>\n<p>This period also saw the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_romantic_nationalism" title="Norwegian romantic nationalism">Norwegian romantic nationalism</a>, as Norwegians sought to define and express a distinct national character.
