The movement covered all branches of culture, including literature (<a href="/wiki/Henrik_Wergeland" title="Henrik Wergeland">Henrik Wergeland</a> [1808\xe2\x80\x931845], <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson" title="Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson">Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson</a> [1832\xe2\x80\x931910], <a href="/wiki/Peter_Christen_Asbj%C3%B8rnsen" title="Peter Christen Asbj\xc3\xb8rnsen">Peter Christen Asbj\xc3\xb8rnsen</a> [1812\xe2\x80\x931845], <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgen_Moe" title="J\xc3\xb8rgen Moe">J\xc3\xb8rgen Moe</a> [1813\xe2\x80\x931882]), painting (<a href="/wiki/Hans_Gude" title="Hans Gude">Hans Gude</a> [1825\xe2\x80\x931903], <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Tidemand" title="Adolph Tidemand">Adolph Tidemand</a> [1814\xe2\x80\x931876]), music (<a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Edvard Grieg</a> [1843\xe2\x80\x931907]), and even language policy, where attempts to define a native written language for Norway led to today\'s two official written forms for Norwegian: <a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokm\xc3\xa5l">Bokm\xc3\xa5l</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_XIV_John_of_Sweden" title="Charles XIV John of Sweden">King Charles III John</a>, who came to the throne of Norway and Sweden in 1818, was the second king following Norway\'s break from Denmark and the union with Sweden. Charles John was a complex man whose long reign extended to 1844. He protected the constitution and liberties of Norway and Sweden during the age of <a href="/wiki/Klemens_Wenzel,_Prince_von_Metternich" class="mw-redirect" title="Klemens Wenzel, Prince von Metternich">Metternich</a>. As such, he was regarded as a liberal monarch for that age. However, he was ruthless in his use of paid informers, the secret police and restrictions on the freedom of the press to put down public movements for reform\xe2\x80\x94especially the Norwegian national independence movement.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic Era</a> that followed the reign of King Charles III John brought some significant social and political reforms. In 1854, women won the right to inherit property in their own right, just like men. In 1863, the last trace of keeping unmarried women in the status of minors was removed. Furthermore, women were then eligible for different occupations, particularly the common school teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> By mid-century, Norway\'s democracy was limited by modern standards: Voting was limited to officials, property owners, leaseholders and burghers of incorporated towns.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saami_Family_1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/220px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/330px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Saami_Family_1900.jpg/440px-Saami_Family_1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3320" data-file-height="2440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saami_Family_1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami</a> family in Norway, c. 1900</div></div></div>\n<p>Still, Norway remained a conservative society. Life in Norway (especially economic life) was "dominated by the aristocracy of professional men who filled most of the important posts in the central government".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> There was no strong bourgeosie class in Norway to demand a breakdown of this aristocratic control of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_Engels_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_Engels-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Thus, even while revolution swept over most of the countries of Europe in 1848, Norway was largely unaffected by revolts that year.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_Engels_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_Engels-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger,_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg/150px-En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg" width="150" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg/225px-En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg/300px-En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger%2C_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:En_brud_fr%C3%A5n_Hardanger,_Norge_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0039996.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bride from <a href="/wiki/Hardanger" title="Hardanger">Hardanger</a>, c. 1900</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Thrane" title="Marcus Thrane">Marcus Thrane</a> was a Utopian socialist. He made his appeal to the labouring classes urging a change of social structure "from below upwards." In 1848, he organised a labour society in <a href="/wiki/Drammen" title="Drammen">Drammen</a>. In just a few months, this society had a membership of 500 and was publishing its own newspaper. Within two years, 300 societies had been organised all over Norway, with a total membership of 20,000 persons. The membership was drawn from the lower classes of both urban and rural areas; for the first time these two groups felt they had a common cause.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In the end, the revolt was easily crushed; Thrane was captured and in 1855, after four years in jail, was sentenced to three additional years for crimes against the safety of the state. Upon his release, Marcus Thrane attempted unsuccessfully to revitalise his movement, but after the death of his wife, he migrated to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1898, all men were granted <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>, followed by all <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women</a> in 1913.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissolution_of_the_union">Dissolution of the union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dissolution of the union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_union_dissolution_referendum,_1905" title="Norwegian union dissolution referendum, 1905">Union dissolution referendum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_union_between_Norway_and_Sweden" title="Dissolution of the union between Norway and Sweden">Dissolution of the union between Norway and Sweden</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_Michelsen" title="Christian Michelsen">Christian Michelsen</a>, a shipping magnate and statesman, and Prime Minister of Norway from 1905 to 1907, played a central role in the peaceful separation of Norway from Sweden on 7 June 1905. A national referendum confirmed the people\'s preference for a monarchy over a republic. No Norwegian could legitimately claim the throne because none was able to prove relationship to medieval royalty and in European tradition <a href="/wiki/Royal_descent" title="Royal descent">royal</a> or "blue" blood is a precondition for laying claim to the throne.\n</p><p>The government offered the throne of Norway to a prince of the Dano-German royal <a href="/wiki/House_of_Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gl%C3%BCcksburg" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gl\xc3\xbccksburg">house of Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gl\xc3\xbccksburg</a>. Prince Carl of Denmark was unanimously elected king by the Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Norway">Parliament</a>, the first king of a fully independent Norway in 508 years (1397: <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a>); he took the name <a href="/wiki/Haakon_VII_of_Norway" title="Haakon VII of Norway">Haakon VII</a>. In 1905, the country welcomed the prince from neighbouring Denmark, his wife <a href="/wiki/Maud_of_Wales" title="Maud of Wales">Maud of Wales</a> and their young son to re-establish Norway\'s royal house. Following centuries of close ties between Norway and Denmark, a prince from the latter was the obvious choice for a European prince who could best relate to the Norwegian people.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_and_Second_World_Wars">First and Second World Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First and Second World Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Norway" title="German occupation of Norway">German occupation of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reichskommissariat_Norwegen" title="Reichskommissariat Norwegen">Reichskommissariat Norwegen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quisling_regime" title="Quisling regime">Quisling regime</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg/220px-Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg/330px-Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg/440px-Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg 2x" data-file-width="699" data-file-height="949" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scenes from the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Campaign" title="Norwegian Campaign">Norwegian Campaign</a> in 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, Norway was in principle a neutral country. In reality, however, Norway had been pressured by the British to hand over increasingly large parts of its large merchant fleet to the British at low rates, as well as to join the trade blockade against Germany. Norwegian merchant marine ships, often with Norwegian sailors still on board, were then sailing under the British flag and at risk of being sunk by German submarines. Thus, many Norwegian sailors and ships were lost. Thereafter, the world ranking of the Norwegian merchant navy fell from fourth place to sixth in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway also proclaimed its neutrality during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, but despite this, it was <a href="/wiki/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung" title="Operation Weser\xc3\xbcbung">invaded by German forces</a> on 9 April 1940. Although Norway was unprepared for the German surprise attack (see: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dr%C3%B8bak_Sound" title="Battle of Dr\xc3\xb8bak Sound">Battle of Dr\xc3\xb8bak Sound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Campaign" title="Norwegian Campaign">Norwegian Campaign</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Norway">Invasion of Norway</a>), military and naval resistance lasted for two months. Norwegian armed forces in the north launched an offensive against the German forces in the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Narvik" title="Battles of Narvik">Battles of Narvik</a>, until they were forced to surrender on 10 June after losing British support which had been diverted to France during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">German invasion of France</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Haakon_VII_of_Norway" title="Haakon VII of Norway">King Haakon</a> and the Norwegian government escaped to <a href="/wiki/Rotherhithe" title="Rotherhithe">Rotherhithe</a> in London. Throughout the war they sent inspirational radio speeches and supported clandestine military actions in Norway against the Germans. On the day of the invasion, the leader of the small National-Socialist party <a href="/wiki/Nasjonal_Samling" title="Nasjonal Samling">Nasjonal Samling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vidkun_Quisling" title="Vidkun Quisling">Vidkun Quisling</a>, tried to seize power, but was forced by the German occupiers to step aside. Real power was wielded by the leader of the German occupation authority, <a href="/wiki/Reichskommissar" title="Reichskommissar">Reichskommissar</a> <a href="/wiki/Josef_Terboven" title="Josef Terboven">Josef Terboven</a>. Quisling, as <i>minister president</i>, later formed a <a href="/wiki/Quisling_regime" title="Quisling regime">collaborationist government under German control</a>. Up to 15,000 Norwegians volunteered to fight in German units, including the <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg/220px-Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg/330px-Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg/440px-Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1586" data-file-height="1333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ole_Friele_Backer_norske_flygere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Norwegian fighter pilots in the United Kingdom during World War II</div></div></div>\n<p>The fraction of the Norwegian population that supported Germany was traditionally smaller than in Sweden, but greater than is generally appreciated today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It included a number of prominent personalities such as <a href="/wiki/Knut_Hamsun" title="Knut Hamsun">Knut Hamsun</a>. The concept of a "Germanic Union" of member states fit well into their thoroughly nationalist-patriotic ideology.\n</p><p>Many Norwegians and persons of Norwegian descent joined the Allied forces as well as the <a href="/wiki/Free_Norwegian_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Norwegian Forces">Free Norwegian Forces</a>. In June 1940, a small group had left Norway following their king to Britain. This group included 13 ships, five aircraft, and 500 men from the Royal Norwegian Navy. By the end of the war, the force had grown to 58 ships and 7,500 men in service in the Royal Norwegian Navy, 5 squadrons of aircraft (including Spitfires, Sunderland flying boats and Mosquitos) in the newly formed Norwegian Air Force, and land forces including the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Independent_Company_1" title="Norwegian Independent Company 1">Norwegian Independent Company 1</a> and 5 Troop as well as No. 10 <a href="/wiki/British_Commandos" class="mw-redirect" title="British Commandos">Commandos</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>During the five years of <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Norway" title="German occupation of Norway">German occupation</a>, Norwegians built a <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_resistance_movement" title="Norwegian resistance movement">resistance movement</a> which fought the German occupation forces with both civil disobedience and armed resistance including the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Norsk_Hydro" title="Norsk Hydro">Norsk Hydro</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> plant and stockpile of heavy water at <a href="/wiki/Vemork" title="Vemork">Vemork</a>, which crippled the German nuclear programme (see: <i><a href="/wiki/Norwegian_heavy_water_sabotage" title="Norwegian heavy water sabotage">Norwegian heavy water sabotage</a></i>). More important to the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> war effort, however, was the role of the Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Merchant Navy">Merchant Marine</a>. At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Invasion" title="Invasion">invasion</a>, Norway had the fourth-largest merchant marine fleet in the world. It was led by the Norwegian shipping company <a href="/wiki/Nortraship" title="Nortraship">Nortraship</a> under the Allies throughout the war and took part in every war operation from the <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">evacuation of Dunkirk</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Normandy_landings" title="Normandy landings">Normandy landings</a>. Each December Norway gives a <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">Christmas tree</a> to the United Kingdom as thanks for the British assistance during the Second World War. A ceremony takes place to erect the tree in London\'s <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II_history">Post-World War II history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Post-World War II history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From 1945 to 1962, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Labour_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Labour Party">Labour Party</a> held an absolute majority in the parliament. The government, led by prime minister <a href="/wiki/Einar_Gerhardsen" title="Einar Gerhardsen">Einar Gerhardsen</a>, embarked on a program inspired by <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, emphasising state financed industrialisation and co-operation between trade unions and <a href="/wiki/Employers%27_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Employers&#39; organisation">employers\' organisations</a>. Many measures of state control of the economy imposed during the war were continued, although the <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">rationing</a> of dairy products was lifted in 1949, while price control and rationing of housing and cars continued as long as until 1960.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StatfjordA(Jarvin1982).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/220px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/330px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/440px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="776" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StatfjordA(Jarvin1982).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Since the 1970s oil production has helped to expand the Norwegian economy and finance the Norwegian state. (<a href="/wiki/Statfjord_oil_field" title="Statfjord oil field">Statfjord oil field</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The wartime alliance with the United Kingdom and the United States was continued in the post-war years. Although pursuing the goal of a socialist economy, the Labour Party distanced itself from the Communists (especially after the Communists\' seizure of power in <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> in 1948), and strengthened its foreign policy and defence policy ties with the US. Norway received <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aid from the United States starting in 1947, joined the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> (OEEC) one year later, and became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO) in 1949.\n</p><p>The first oil was discovered at the small Balder field in 1967, production only began in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 1969, the <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Petroleum_Company" title="Phillips Petroleum Company">Phillips Petroleum Company</a> discovered petroleum resources at the <a href="/wiki/Ekofisk_oil_field" title="Ekofisk oil field">Ekofisk</a> field west of Norway. In 1973, the Norwegian government founded the State oil company, <a href="/wiki/Statoil" class="mw-redirect" title="Statoil">Statoil</a>. Oil production did not provide net income until the early 1980s because of the large capital investment that was required to establish the country\'s petroleum industry. Around 1975, both the proportion and absolute number of workers in industry peaked. Since then labour-intensive industries and services like factory mass production and shipping have largely been outsourced.\n</p><p>Norway was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a> (EFTA). Norway was twice invited to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, but ultimately declined to join after referendums that failed by narrow margins in 1972 and 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg/220px-2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg/330px-2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg/440px-2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011_Norway_attacks_flower_march_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Town Hall Square in Oslo filled with people with roses mourning the victims of the <a href="/wiki/2011_Norway_attacks#Ut\xc3\xb8ya_massacre" title="2011 Norway attacks">Ut\xc3\xb8ya massacre</a>, 22 July 2011</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1981, a Conservative government led by <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A5re_Willoch" title="K\xc3\xa5re Willoch">K\xc3\xa5re Willoch</a> replaced the Labour Party with a policy of stimulating the <a href="/wiki/Stagflation" title="Stagflation">stagflated economy</a> with tax cuts, economic liberalisation, deregulation of markets, and measures to curb record-high inflation (13.6% in 1981).\n</p><p>Norway\'s first female prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Gro_Harlem_Brundtland" title="Gro Harlem Brundtland">Gro Harlem Brundtland</a> of the Labour party, continued many of the reforms of her conservative predecessor, while backing traditional Labour concerns such as <a href="/wiki/Social_security" title="Social security">social security</a>, high taxes, the industrialisation of nature, and feminism. By the late 1990s, Norway had paid off its foreign debt and had started accumulating a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_wealth_fund" title="Sovereign wealth fund">sovereign wealth fund</a>. Since the 1990s, a divisive question in politics has been how much of the income from petroleum production the government should spend, and how much it should save.\n</p><p>In 2011, Norway suffered <a href="/wiki/2011_Norway_attacks" title="2011 Norway attacks">two terrorist attacks</a> on the same day conducted by <a href="/wiki/Anders_Behring_Breivik" title="Anders Behring Breivik">Anders Behring Breivik</a> which struck the <a href="/wiki/Regjeringskvartalet" title="Regjeringskvartalet">government quarter</a> in Oslo and a summer camp of the Labour party\'s <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_Youth_League_(Norway)" title="Workers&#39; Youth League (Norway)">youth movement</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ut%C3%B8ya" title="Ut\xc3\xb8ya">Ut\xc3\xb8ya</a> island, resulting in 77 deaths and 319 wounded.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_parliamentary_election,_2013" title="Norwegian parliamentary election, 2013">2013 Norwegian parliamentary election</a> brought a more conservative government to power, with the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(Norway)" title="Conservative Party (Norway)">Conservative Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Progress_Party_(Norway)" title="Progress Party (Norway)">Progress Party</a> winning 43% of the electorate\'s votes.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Norway" title="Geography of Norway">Geography of Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Norway" title="Geology of Norway">Geology of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg/220px-Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg/330px-Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg/440px-Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4960" data-file-height="5820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Norway_in_February_2003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A satellite image of continental Norway in winter</div></div></div>\n<p>Norway\'s core territory comprises the western and northernmost portion of the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Peninsula" title="Scandinavian Peninsula">Scandinavian Peninsula</a>; the remote island of <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> and the archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> are also part of the Kingdom of Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> The Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_Island" title="Peter I Island">Peter I Island</a> and the sub-Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a> are <a href="/wiki/Dependencies_of_Norway" title="Dependencies of Norway">dependent territories</a> and thus not considered part of the Kingdom. Norway also lays claim to a section of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">Queen Maud Land</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Norge_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norge-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> From the Middle ages to 1814 Norway was part of the <a href="/wiki/Denmark%E2%80%93Norway" title="Denmark\xe2\x80\x93Norway">Danish kingdom</a>. Norwegian possessions in the North Atlantic, <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, remained Danish when Norway was passed to Sweden at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kiel" title="Treaty of Kiel">Treaty of Kiel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Norway also comprised <a href="/wiki/Bohusl%C3%A4n" title="Bohusl\xc3\xa4n">Bohusl\xc3\xa4n</a> until 1658, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A4mtland" title="J\xc3\xa4mtland">J\xc3\xa4mtland</a> and <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4rjedalen" title="H\xc3\xa4rjedalen">H\xc3\xa4rjedalen</a> until 1645,<sup id="cite_ref-Norge_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norge-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> until 1468,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> in 1266.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet,_2013_June.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg/220px-Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg/330px-Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg/440px-Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet%2C_2013_June.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2405" data-file-height="3600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trollryggen_and_Trollveggen_from_Litlefjellet,_2013_June.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tallest vertical rock face in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Trollveggen" class="mw-redirect" title="Trollveggen">Trollveggen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trollryggen" title="Trollryggen">Trollryggen</a> over the river <a href="/wiki/Rauma_(river)" title="Rauma (river)">Rauma</a> in <a href="/wiki/Romsdalen" title="Romsdalen">Romsdalen</a> valley.</div></div></div>\n<p>Norway comprises the western and northernmost part of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> in Northern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-SNL_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SNL-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Norway lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/57th_parallel_north" title="57th parallel north">57\xc2\xb0</a> and <a href="/wiki/81st_parallel_north" title="81st parallel north">81\xc2\xb0 N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/4th_meridian_east" title="4th meridian east">4\xc2\xb0</a> and <a href="/wiki/32nd_meridian_east" title="32nd meridian east">32\xc2\xb0 E</a>. Norway is the northernmost of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> and if Svalbard is included also the easternmost.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vard%C3%B8" title="Vard\xc3\xb8">Vard\xc3\xb8</a> at 31\xc2\xb0 10\' 07" east of Greenwich lies further east than St. Petersburg and Istanbul.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Norway includes the northernmost point on the European mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The rugged coastline is broken by huge <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> and thousands of islands. The coastal <a href="/wiki/Baseline_(sea)" title="Baseline (sea)">baseline</a> is 2,532 kilometres (1,573&#160;mi). The coastline of the mainland including fjords stretches 28,953 kilometres (17,991&#160;mi), when islands are included the coastline has been estimated to 100,915 kilometres (62,706&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Norway shares a 1,619-kilometre (1,006&#160;mi) land border with <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, 727 kilometres (452&#160;mi) with <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, and 196 kilometres (122&#160;mi) with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the east. To the north, west and south, Norway is bordered by the <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Mountains" title="Scandinavian Mountains">Scandinavian Mountains</a> form much of the border with Sweden.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG/220px-Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG/330px-Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG/440px-Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reine_Lofoten_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reine" title="Reine">Reine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lofoten" title="Lofoten">Lofoten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Norway" title="Northern Norway">Northern Norway</a></div></div></div>\n<p>At 385,252 square kilometres (148,747&#160;sq&#160;mi) (including <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a>) (and 323,802 square kilometres (125,021&#160;sq&#160;mi) without), much of the country is dominated by mountainous or high terrain, with a great variety of natural features caused by prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and varied <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>. The most noticeable of these are the fjords: deep grooves cut into the land flooded by the sea following the end of the Ice Age. <a href="/wiki/Sognefjord" title="Sognefjord">Sognefjorden</a> is the world\'s second deepest fjord, and the world\'s longest at 204 kilometres (127&#160;mi). <a href="/wiki/Hornindalsvatnet" title="Hornindalsvatnet">Hornindalsvatnet</a> is the deepest lake in all Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Norway has about 400,000 lakes.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> There are registred 239,057 islands.<sup id="cite_ref-SNL_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SNL-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">Permafrost</a> can be found all year in the higher mountain areas and in the interior of Finnmark county. <a href="/wiki/List_of_glaciers_in_Norway" title="List of glaciers in Norway">Numerous glaciers</a> are found in Norway.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg/220px-The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg/330px-The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg/440px-The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Troll%27s_Tongue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ringedalsvatnet" title="Ringedalsvatnet">Ringedalsvatnet</a> lake and <a href="/wiki/Trolltunga" title="Trolltunga">Trolltunga</a> cliff</div></div></div>\n<p>The land is mostly made of hard <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gneiss" title="Gneiss">gneiss</a> rock, but <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> are also common, and the lowest elevations contain marine deposits. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and prevailing westerlies, Norway experiences higher temperatures and more precipitation than expected at such northern latitudes, especially along the coast. The mainland experiences four distinct seasons, with colder winters and less precipitation inland. The northernmost part has a mostly maritime <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">Subarctic climate</a>, while Svalbard has an <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> climate.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg/220px-Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg/330px-Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg/440px-Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1408" data-file-height="1056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaulosen_og_%C3%98ysand.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Norwegian lowland landscape near the Gaulosen branch of <a href="/wiki/Trondheimsfjord" class="mw-redirect" title="Trondheimsfjord">Trondheimsfjord</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Because of the large latitudinal range of the country and the varied topography and climate, Norway has a larger number of different <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> than almost any other European country. There are approximately 60,000 species in Norway and adjacent waters (excluding bacteria and virus). The Norwegian Shelf large marine ecosystem is considered highly productive.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Langeby_Camping,_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg/220px-Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg/330px-Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg/440px-Langeby_Camping%2C_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8393" data-file-height="6081" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Langeby_Camping,_Sandefjord._-_no-nb_digifoto_20160511_00221_NB_NKF_P_02_E_026.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sandefjord" title="Sandefjord">Sandefjord</a> is a resort town which has Norway\'s highest number of annual cloud-free days.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><br /><small><a href="/wiki/Langeby" title="Langeby">Langeby Beach</a>, 1966.</small></div></div></div>\n<p>The southern and western parts of Norway, fully exposed to Atlantic storm fronts, experience more precipitation and have milder winters than the eastern and far northern parts. Areas to the east of the coastal mountains are in a <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a>, and have lower rain and snow totals than the west. The lowlands around Oslo have the warmest and sunniest summers, but also cold weather and snow in wintertime.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwegian-Meteorological-Institute_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwegian-Meteorological-Institute-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Book-Climate_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Climate-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of Norway\'s high <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, there are large seasonal variations in daylight. From late May to late July, the sun never completely descends beneath the horizon in areas north of the Arctic Circle (hence Norway\'s description as the "Land of the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">Midnight sun</a>"), and the rest of the country experiences up to 20 hours of daylight per day. Conversely, from late November to late January, the sun never rises above the horizon in the north, and daylight hours are very short in the rest of the country.\n</p><p>The coastal climate of Norway is exceptionally mild compared with areas on similar latitudes elsewhere in the world, with the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> passing directly offshore the northern areas of the Atlantic coast, continuously warming the region in the winter. Temperature anomalies found in coastal locations are exceptional, with <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8st" title="R\xc3\xb8st">R\xc3\xb8st</a> and <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A6r%C3%B8y" title="V\xc3\xa6r\xc3\xb8y">V\xc3\xa6r\xc3\xb8y</a> lacking a meteorological winter in spite of being north of the Arctic Circle. The Gulf Stream has this effect only on the northern parts of Norway, not in the south, despite what is commonly believed. The northern coast of Norway would thus be ice-covered if not for the Gulf Stream.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> As a side-effect, the Scandinavian Mountains prevent continental winds from reaching the coastline, causing very cool summers throughout Atlantic Norway. Oslo has more of a continental climate, similar to Sweden\'s. The mountain ranges have subarctic and tundra climates. There is also very high rainfall in areas exposed to the Atlantic, such as Bergen. Oslo, in comparison, is dry, being in a <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a>. <a href="/wiki/Skj%C3%A5k" title="Skj\xc3\xa5k">Skj\xc3\xa5k</a> in Oppland county is also in the rain shadow and is one of the driest places with 278 millimetres (10.9 inches) precipitation annually. <a href="/wiki/Finnmarksvidda" title="Finnmarksvidda">Finnmarksvidda</a> and the interior valleys of <a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nordland" title="Nordland">Nordland</a> also receive less than 300 millimetres (12 inches) annually. Longyearbyen is the driest place in Norway with 190 millimetres (7.5 inches).<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parts of southeastern Norway including parts of <a href="/wiki/Mj%C3%B8sa" title="Mj\xc3\xb8sa">Mj\xc3\xb8sa</a> have warm-summer <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climates</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen</a> Dfb), while the more southern and western coasts are mostly of the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (Cfb). Further inland in southeastern and northern Norway, the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subarctic climate</a> (Dfc) dominates; this is especially true for areas in the rain shadow of the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Mountains" title="Scandinavian Mountains">Scandinavian Mountains</a>. Some of the inner valleys of <a href="/wiki/Oppland" title="Oppland">Oppland</a> get so little precipitation annually, thanks to the rain shadow effect, that they meet the requirements for dry-summer subarctic climates (Dsc). In higher altitudes, close to the coasts of southern and western Norway, one can find the rare subpolar oceanic climate (Cfc). This climate is also common in Northern Norway, but there usually in lower altitudes, all the way down to sea level. A small part of the northernmost coast of Norway has the <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">tundra/alpine/polar climate</a> (ET). Large parts of Norway are covered by mountains and high altitude plateaus, many of which also exhibit the <a href="/wiki/Tundra_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tundra climate">tundra/alpine/polar climate</a> (ET).<sup id="cite_ref-Norwegian-Meteorological-Institute_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwegian-Meteorological-Institute-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Book-Climate_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Climate-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="background: #A2FFA2; color:#000000;">62<br /> (2.44)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #86FF86; color:#000000;">78<br /> (3.07)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #78FF78; color:#000000;">90<br /> (3.54)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #89FF89; color:#000000;">79<br /> (3.11)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #7CFF7C; color:#000000;">85<br /> (3.35)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #87FF87; color:#000000;">80<br /> (3.15)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #97FF97; color:#000000;">67<br /> (2.64)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #ADFFAD; color:#000000;">55<br /> (2.17)\n</td>\n<td style="background: #9EFF9E; color:#000000; border-left-width:medium">766<br /> (30.16)\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n\n<tr>\n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: <sup id="cite_ref-Meteklima_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meteklima-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arcticfox-3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Arcticfox-3.jpg/220px-Arcticfox-3.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Arcticfox-3.jpg/330px-Arcticfox-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Arcticfox-3.jpg/440px-Arcticfox-3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arcticfox-3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a> has its habitat in high elevation ranges on the mainland as well as on <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The total number of species include 16,000 species of insects (probably 4,000 more species yet to be described), 20,000 species of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, 1,800 species of <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a>, 1,050 species of <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>, 2,800 species of <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>, up to 7,000 species of <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, 450 species of birds (250 species nesting in Norway), 90 species of mammals, 45 fresh-water species of fish, 150 salt-water species of fish, 1,000 species of fresh-water <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, and 3,500 species of salt-water invertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> About 40,000 of these species have been described by science. The <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">red list</a> of 2010 encompasses 4,599 species.<sup id="cite_ref-red_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-red-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joonis1.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Joonis1.tif/lossless-page1-220px-Joonis1.tif.png" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Joonis1.tif/lossless-page1-330px-Joonis1.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Joonis1.tif/lossless-page1-440px-Joonis1.tif.png 2x" data-file-width="1581" data-file-height="988" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joonis1.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cold-water <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> in <a href="/wiki/Skagerak" class="mw-redirect" title="Skagerak">Skagerak</a> off of the southern coast of Norway.</div></div></div>\n<p>Seventeen species are listed mainly because they are endangered on a global scale, such as the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_beaver" title="Eurasian beaver">European beaver</a>, even if the population in Norway is not seen as endangered. The number of threatened and near-threatened species equals to 3,682; it includes 418 fungi species, many of which are closely associated with the small remaining areas of old-growth forests,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> 36 bird species, and 16 species of mammals. In 2010, 2,398 species were listed as endangered or vulnerable; of these were 1250 listed as vulnerable (VU), 871 as endangered (EN), and 276 species as critically endangered (CR), among which were the <a href="/wiki/Grey_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey wolf">grey wolf</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a> (healthy population on Svalbard) and the <a href="/wiki/Pool_frog" title="Pool frog">pool frog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-red_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-red-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest predator in Norwegian waters is the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a>, and the largest fish is the <a href="/wiki/Basking_shark" title="Basking shark">basking shark</a>. The largest predator on land is the <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bear</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a> is the largest predator on the Norwegian mainland. The largest land animal on the mainland is the elk (American English: <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a>). The elk in Norway is known for its size and strength and is often called <i>skogens konge</i>, "king of the forest".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Attractive and dramatic scenery and landscape are found throughout Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The west coast of southern Norway and the coast of northern Norway present some of the most visually impressive coastal sceneries in the world. <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic</a> has listed the Norwegian fjords as the world\'s top tourist attraction.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The country is also home to the natural phenomena of the Midnight sun (during summer), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">Aurora borealis</a> known also as the Northern lights.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2016 <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">Environmental Performance Index</a> from <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> put Norway in seventeenth place, immediately below Croatia and Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-epi_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epi-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The index is based on environmental risks to human health, habitat loss, and changes in CO2 emissions. The index notes over-exploitation of fisheries, but not <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_Norway" title="Whaling in Norway">Norway\'s whaling</a> or <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Norway" title="Energy in Norway">oil exports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-indi_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indi-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:center; max-width:1050px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Summer_evening_at_Lovatnet,_Stryn,_Norway,_2013_June_-_3.jpg" class="image" title="A summer evening view from Sande over Lovatnet in Stryn, Sogn og Fjordane, in 2013"><img alt="" href="/wiki/Stryn" title="Stryn">Stryn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogn_og_Fjordane" title="Sogn og Fjordane">Sogn og Fjordane</a>, in 2013</div>\n</div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_government">Politics and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Politics and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Norway" title="Politics of Norway">Politics of Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Norway" title="Law of Norway">Law of Norway</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_parliamentary_election,_2017" title="Norwegian parliamentary election, 2017">Norwegian parliamentary election, 2017</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg/220px-Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg/330px-Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg/440px-Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3136" data-file-height="1764" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Det_Kongelige_Slott_85500_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Palace,_Oslo" title="Royal Palace, Oslo">The Royal Palace</a> in Oslo.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG/220px-Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG" width="220" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG/440px-Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3270" data-file-height="2331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kong_Harald_og_Dronning_Sonja_-_Kino_2012.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harald_V_of_Norway" title="Harald V of Norway">King Harald V</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sonja_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonja of Norway">Queen Sonja</a> of Norway (reigning since 1991) in 2012.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Storting_Spring_2016.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Storting_Spring_2016.JPG/220px-Storting_Spring_2016.JPG" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Storting_Spring_2016.JPG/330px-Storting_Spring_2016.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Storting_Spring_2016.JPG/440px-Storting_Spring_2016.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2922" data-file-height="1783" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Storting_Spring_2016.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Storting_building" title="Storting building">The Storting</a></i> is the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Norway">Parliament of Norway</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg;_January_2018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg/220px-President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg/330px-President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg/440px-President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg%3B_January_2018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Donald_Trump_and_Prime_Minister_Erna_Solberg;_January_2018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Norway" title="Prime Minister of Norway">Prime Minister</a> of Norway <a href="/wiki/Erna_Solberg" title="Erna Solberg">Erna Solberg</a> (since 2013) and U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> in 2018.</div></div></div>\n<p>Norway is considered to be one of the most developed democracies and <a href="/wiki/Rechtsstaat" title="Rechtsstaat">states of justice</a> in the world. From 1814, c. 45% of men (25 years and older) had the right to vote, whereas the United Kingdom had c. 20% (1832), Sweden c. 5% (1866), and Belgium c. 1.15% (1840). Since 2010, Norway has been classified as the world\'s most democratic country by the <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Constitution of Norway</a>, which was adopted on 17 May 1814<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and inspired by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of 1776 and 1789, respectively, Norway is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary system</a> of government, wherein the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Norway" title="Monarchy of Norway">King of Norway</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Norway" title="Prime Minister of Norway">prime minister</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. Power is separated among the legislative, executive and judicial branches of government, as defined by the Constitution, which serves as the country\'s supreme legal document.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Royal Family">monarch</a> officially retains executive power. But following the introduction of a parliamentary system of government, the duties of the monarch have since become strictly representative and ceremonial,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> such as the formal appointment and dismissal of the Prime Minister and other ministers in the executive government. Accordingly, the Monarch is <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Armed_Forces" title="Norwegian Armed Forces">Norwegian Armed Forces</a>, and serves as chief diplomatic official abroad and as a symbol of unity. <a href="/wiki/Harald_V_of_Norway" title="Harald V of Norway">Harald V</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Gl%C3%BCcksburg" title="House of Gl\xc3\xbccksburg">House of Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gl\xc3\xbccksburg</a> was crowned King of Norway in 1991, the first since the 14th century who has been born in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-norway.org_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway.org-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haakon,_Crown_Prince_of_Norway" title="Haakon, Crown Prince of Norway">Haakon, Crown Prince of Norway</a>, is the legal and rightful heir to the throne and the Kingdom.\n</p><p>In practice, the Prime Minister exercises the executive powers. Constitutionally, legislative power is vested with both the government and the Parliament of Norway, but the latter is the supreme legislature and a <a href="/wiki/Unicameralism" title="Unicameralism">unicameral</a> body.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Norway is fundamentally structured as a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>. The Parliament can pass a law by simple majority of the 169 representatives, who are elected on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> from 19 constituencies for four-year terms.\n</p><p>150 are elected directly from the 19 constituencies, and an additional 19 seats ("levelling seats") are allocated on a nationwide basis to make the representation in parliament correspond better with the popular vote for the political parties. A 4% election threshold is required for a party to gain levelling seats in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Election_threshold_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Election_threshold-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> There are a total of 169 members of parliament.\n</p><p>The Parliament of Norway, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Norway">Stortinget</a></i> (meaning Grand Assembly), ratifies national <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaties</a> developed by the executive branch. It can <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeach</a> members of the government if their acts are declared unconstitutional. If an indicted suspect is impeached, Parliament has the power to remove the person from office.\n</p><p>The position of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Norway" title="Prime Minister of Norway">prime minister</a>, Norway\'s head of government, is allocated to the member of Parliament who can obtain the confidence of a majority in Parliament, usually the current leader of the largest political party or, more effectively, through a coalition of parties. A single party generally does not have sufficient political power in terms of the number of seats to form a government on its own. Norway has often been ruled by minority governments.\n</p><p>The prime minister nominates the cabinet, traditionally drawn from members of the same political party or parties in the Storting, making up the government. The PM organises the executive government and exercises its power as vested by the Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Norway has a state church, the Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Norway" title="Church of Norway">Church of Norway</a>, which has in recent years gradually been granted more internal autonomy in day-to-day affairs, but which still has a special constitutional status. Formerly, the PM had to have more than half the members of cabinet be members of the Church of Norway, meaning at least ten out of the 19 ministries. This rule was however removed in 2012. The issue of <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a> in Norway has been increasingly controversial, as many people believe it is time to change this, to reflect the growing diversity in the population. A part of this is the evolution of the public school subject Christianity, a required subject since 1739. Even the state\'s loss in a battle at the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> at <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> in 2007 did not settle the matter. As of 1 January 2017, the Church of Norway is a separate legal entity, and no longer a branch of the civil service.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Council_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Council of State">Council of State</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Privy_council" title="Privy council">privy council</a> presided over by the monarch, the prime minister and the cabinet meet at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Palace,_Oslo" title="Royal Palace, Oslo">Royal Palace</a> and formally consult the Monarch. All government bills need the formal approval by the monarch before and after introduction to Parliament. The Council reviews and approves all of the monarch\'s actions as head of state. Although all government and parliamentary acts are decided beforehand, the privy council is an example of symbolic gesture the king retains.<sup id="cite_ref-norway.org_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway.org-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Members of the Storting are directly elected from <a href="/wiki/Party-list_proportional_representation" title="Party-list proportional representation">party-lists</a> <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> in nineteen <a href="/wiki/Plurality_voting_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Plurality voting system">plural-member</a> constituencies in a national <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Historically, both the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Labour_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Labour Party">Norwegian Labour Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(Norway)" title="Conservative Party (Norway)">Conservative Party</a> have played leading political roles. In the early 21st century, the Labour Party has been in power since the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_parliamentary_election,_2005" title="Norwegian parliamentary election, 2005">2005 election</a>, in a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Red%E2%80%93Green_Coalition&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Red\xe2\x80\x93Green Coalition (page does not exist)">Red\xe2\x80\x93Green Coalition</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Left_Party_(Norway)" title="Socialist Left Party (Norway)">Socialist Left Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Norway)" title="Centre Party (Norway)">Centre Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 2005, both the Conservative Party and the <a href="/wiki/Progress_Party_(Norway)" title="Progress Party (Norway)">Progress Party</a> have won numerous seats in the Parliament, but not sufficient in the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_parliamentary_election,_2009" title="Norwegian parliamentary election, 2009">2009 general election</a> to overthrow the coalition. Commentators have pointed to the poor co-operation between the opposition parties, including the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Norway)" title="Liberal Party (Norway)">Liberals</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Party_(Norway)" title="Christian Democratic Party (Norway)">Christian Democrats</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jens_Stoltenberg" title="Jens Stoltenberg">Jens Stoltenberg</a>, the leader of the Labour Party, continued to have the necessary majority through his multi-party alliance to continue as PM until 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In national elections in September 2013, voters ended eight years of Labor rule. Two political parties, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B8yre" class="mw-redirect" title="H\xc3\xb8yre">H\xc3\xb8yre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_Party_(Norway)" title="Progress Party (Norway)">Fremskrittspartiet</a>, elected on promises of tax cuts, more spending on infrastructure and education, better services and stricter rules on immigration, formed a government. Coming at a time when Norway\'s economy is in good condition with low unemployment, the rise of the right appeared to be based on other issues. <a href="/wiki/Erna_Solberg" title="Erna Solberg">Erna Solberg</a> became prime minister, the second female prime minister after <a href="/wiki/Gro_Harlem_Brundtland" title="Gro Harlem Brundtland">Brundtland</a> and the first conservative prime minister since <a href="/wiki/Jan_P._Syse" title="Jan P. Syse">Syse</a>. Solberg said her win was "a historic election victory for the right-wing parties".<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Norway" title="Administrative divisions of Norway">Administrative divisions of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Norway" title="Counties of Norway">Counties of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipalities of Norway">Municipalities of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_and_cities_in_Norway" title="List of towns and cities in Norway">List of towns and cities in Norway</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dependencies_of_Norway" title="Dependencies of Norway">Dependencies of Norway</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi_(area)" class="mw-redirect" title="S\xc3\xa1pmi (area)">S\xc3\xa1pmi (area)</a></div>\n<p>Norway, a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary state</a>, is divided into eighteen first-level administrative <a href="/wiki/County" title="County">counties</a> (<i>fylke</i>). The counties are administrated through directly elected county assemblies who elect the County Governor. Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Royal Family">King</a> and government are represented in every county by a <a href="/wiki/County_governor_(Norway)" title="County governor (Norway)">fylkesmann</a>, who effectively acts as a Governor.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> As such, the Government is directly represented at a local level through the County Governors\' offices. The counties are then sub-divided into 422-second-level municipalities (<i>kommuner</i>), which in turn are administrated by directly elected municipal council, headed by a mayor and a small executive cabinet. The capital of <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a> is considered both a county and a municipality.\n</p><p>Norway has two integral overseas territories: <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, the only developed island in the archipelago of the same name, located miles away to the north. There are three <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subantarctic" title="Subantarctic">Subantarctic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dependent_territory" title="Dependent territory">dependencies</a>: <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_Island" title="Peter I Island">Peter I Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">Queen Maud Land</a>. On most maps, there had been an unclaimed area between Queen Maud Land and the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> until 12 June 2015 when Norway formally annexed that area.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Norway_political-geo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_Norway_political-geo.png/300px-Map_Norway_political-geo.png" width="300" height="453" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_Norway_political-geo.png/450px-Map_Norway_political-geo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_Norway_political-geo.png/600px-Map_Norway_political-geo.png 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2902" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Norway_political-geo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A geopolitical map of Norway, showing the 19 <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Norway" title="Counties of Norway">fylker</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a> (Spitsbergen) and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> islands, which are part of the Norwegian kingdom</div></div></div>\n<p>96 settlements have <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_and_cities_in_Norway" title="List of towns and cities in Norway">city</a> status in Norway. In most cases, the city borders are coterminous with the borders of their respective municipalities. Often, Norwegian city municipalities include large areas that are not developed; for example, Oslo municipality contains large forests, located north and south-east of the city, and over half of Bergen municipality consists of mountainous areas.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a
