href="/wiki/Hordaland" title="Hordaland">Hordaland</a></td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">23,194\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_system_and_law_enforcement">Judicial system and law enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Judicial system and law enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Norway" title="Judiciary of Norway">Judiciary of Norway</a></div>\n<p>Norway uses a <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law system</a> where laws are created and amended in Parliament and the system regulated through the <a href="/wiki/Courts_of_justice_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Courts of justice of Norway">Courts of justice of Norway</a>. It consists of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Norway" title="Supreme Court of Norway">Supreme Court</a> of 20 permanent judges and a <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_Norway" title="Chief Justice of the Supreme Court of Norway">Chief Justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Appellate_court" title="Appellate court">appellate courts</a>, city and <a href="/wiki/Courts_of_justice_of_Norway#District_courts" class="mw-redirect" title="Courts of justice of Norway">district courts</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Conciliation_councils&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Conciliation councils (page does not exist)">conciliation councils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-norway_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The judiciary is independent of executive and legislative branches. While the Prime Minister nominates Supreme Court Justices for office, their nomination must be approved by Parliament and formally confirmed by the Monarch in the Council of State. Usually, judges attached to regular courts are formally appointed by the Monarch on the advice of the Prime Minister.\n</p><p>The Courts\' strict and formal mission is to regulate the Norwegian judicial system, interpret the Constitution, and as such implement the legislation adopted by Parliament. In its judicial reviews, it monitors the legislative and executive branches to ensure that they comply with provisions of enacted legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-norway_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Law enforcement in Norway">law is enforced in Norway</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Police_Service" title="Norwegian Police Service">Norwegian Police Service</a>. It is a Unified National Police Service made up of 27 Police Districts and several specialist agencies, such as <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_National_Authority_for_the_Investigation_and_Prosecution_of_Economic_and_Environmental_Crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian National Authority for the Investigation and Prosecution of Economic and Environmental Crime">Norwegian National Authority for the Investigation and Prosecution of Economic and Environmental Crime</a>, known as <i>\xc3\x98kokrim</i>; and the <a href="/wiki/National_Criminal_Investigation_Service_(Norway)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Criminal Investigation Service (Norway)">National Criminal Investigation Service</a>, known as <i>Kripos</i>, each headed by a chief of police. The Police Service is headed by the <a href="/wiki/National_Police_Directorate" class="mw-redirect" title="National Police Directorate">National Police Directorate</a>, which reports to the Ministry of Justice and the Police. The Police Directorate is headed by a National Police Commissioner. The only exception is the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Police_Security_Service" title="Norwegian Police Security Service">Norwegian Police Security Agency</a>, whose head answers directly to the Ministry of Justice and the Police.\n</p><p>Norway abolished the death penalty for regular criminal acts in 1902. The legislature abolished the death penalty for high treason in war and war-crimes in 1979. <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a>, in its 2007 Worldwide Press Freedom Index, ranked Norway at a shared first place (along with Iceland) out of 169 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In general, the legal and institutional framework in Norway is characterised by a high degree of transparency, accountability and integrity, and the perception and the occurrence of corruption are very low.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Norway has ratified all relevant international anti-corruption conventions, and its standards of implementation and enforcement of anti-corruption legislation are considered very high by many international anti-corruption working groups such as the OECD Anti-Bribery Working Group. However, there are some isolated cases showing that some municipalities have abused their position in public procurement processes.\n</p><p>Norwegian prisons are humane, rather than tough, with emphasis on rehabilitation. At 20%, Norway\'s re-conviction rate is among the lowest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Norway" title="Foreign relations of Norway">Foreign relations of Norway</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Norway_and_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Norway and the European Union">Norway and the European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_Norway" title="Whaling in Norway">Whaling in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg/220px-KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg/330px-KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg/440px-KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KNM_Fridtjof_Nansen-2006-06-01-side.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Norwegian_Navy" title="Royal Norwegian Navy">Royal Norwegian Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen-class_frigate" title="Fridtjof Nansen-class frigate"><i>Fridtjof Nansen</i> class frigate</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Norway maintains embassies in 82 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> 60 countries maintain an embassy in Norway, all of them in the capital, Oslo.\n</p><p>Norway is a founding member of the United Nations (UN), the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO), the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA). Norway issued applications for accession to the European Union (EU) and its predecessors in 1962, 1967 and 1992, respectively. While Denmark, Sweden and Finland obtained membership, the Norwegian electorate rejected the treaties of accession in referenda in 1972 and 1994.\n</p><p>After the 1994 referendum, Norway maintained its membership in the European Economic Area (EEA), an arrangement granting the country access to the <a href="/wiki/Single_market_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Single market of the European Union">internal market</a> of the Union, on the condition that Norway implements the Union\'s pieces of legislation which are deemed relevant (of which there were approximately seven thousand by 2010)<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Successive Norwegian governments have, since 1994, requested participation in parts of the EU\'s co-operation that go beyond the provisions of the EEA agreement. Non-voting participation by Norway has been granted in, for instance, the Union\'s <a href="/wiki/Common_Security_and_Defence_Policy" title="Common Security and Defence Policy">Common Security and Defence Policy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Agreement" title="Schengen Agreement">Schengen Agreement</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Defence_Agency" title="European Defence Agency">European Defence Agency</a>, as well as 19 separate programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway contributes to <a href="/wiki/International_development" title="International development">international development</a>. In addition, it participated in the 1990s brokering of the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Accords" title="Oslo Accords">Oslo Accords</a>, an attempt to resolve the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict">Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict</a>. These were unsuccessful.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Armed_Forces" title="Norwegian Armed Forces">Norwegian Armed Forces</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg/220px-2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg/330px-2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg/440px-2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2860" data-file-height="1910" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2_norwegian_Leopard_tanks_in_the_snow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Leopard_1" title="Leopard 1">Leopard</a> tanks in the snow in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A5lselv" title="M\xc3\xa5lselv">M\xc3\xa5lselv</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Norwegian Armed Forces numbers about 25,000 personnel, including civilian employees. According to 2009 mobilisation plans, full mobilisation produces approximately 83,000 combatant personnel. Norway has <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> (including 6\xe2\x80\x9312 months of training);<sup id="cite_ref-NDFnumbers_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDFnumbers-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> in 2013, the country became the first in Europe and NATO to draft women as well as men. However, due to less need for conscripts after the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> ended with the break-up of the Soviet Union, few people have to serve if they are not motivated.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The Armed Forces are subordinate to the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Ministry_of_Defence" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Ministry of Defence">Norwegian Ministry of Defence</a>. The Commander-in-Chief is <a href="/wiki/Harald_V_of_Norway" title="Harald V of Norway">King Harald V</a>. The military of Norway is divided into the following branches: the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Army" title="Norwegian Army">Norwegian Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Norwegian_Navy" title="Royal Norwegian Navy">Royal Norwegian Navy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Norwegian_Air_Force" title="Royal Norwegian Air Force">Royal Norwegian Air Force</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Cyber_Defence_Force" title="Norwegian Cyber Defence Force">Norwegian Cyber Defence Force</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Home_Guard_(Norway)" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Guard (Norway)">Home Guard</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Norwegian_F-35.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/First_Norwegian_F-35.png/220px-First_Norwegian_F-35.png" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/First_Norwegian_F-35.png/330px-First_Norwegian_F-35.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/First_Norwegian_F-35.png/440px-First_Norwegian_F-35.png 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Norwegian_F-35.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first Norwegian <a href="/wiki/F-35_Lightning_II" class="mw-redirect" title="F-35 Lightning II">F-35 Lightning II</a> lands at Luke Air Force Base</div></div></div>\n<p>In response to its being overrun by Germany in 1940, the country was one of the founding nations of the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO) on 4 April 1949. At present, Norway contributes in the <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF) in <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Norway has contributed in several missions in contexts of the United Nations, NATO, and the <a href="/wiki/Common_Security_and_Defence_Policy" title="Common Security and Defence Policy">Common Security and Defence Policy</a> of the European Union.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Norway" title="Economy of Norway">Economy of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Norway" title="Energy in Norway">Energy in Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Area" title="European Economic Area">European Economic Area</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone#Norway" title="Exclusive economic zone">Exclusive economic zone \xc2\xa7&#160;Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Norway_treemap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norway_treemap.png/300px-Norway_treemap.png" width="300" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norway_treemap.png/450px-Norway_treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norway_treemap.png/600px-Norway_treemap.png 2x" data-file-width="1108" data-file-height="867" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Norway_treemap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphical depiction of Norway\'s product exports in 28 colour-coded categories.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG/300px-GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG" width="300" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG/450px-GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG/600px-GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="569" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GDP_Norway_1865_to_2004.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>GDP and GDP growth</div></div></div>\n<p>Norwegians enjoy the second-highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita">GDP per-capita</a> among European countries (after <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>), and the sixth-highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP (PPP) per-capita</a> in the world. Today, Norway ranks as the second-wealthiest country in the world in monetary value, with the largest capital reserve per capita of any nation.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> According to the CIA World Factbook, Norway is a net external creditor of debt.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Norway maintained first place in the world in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">UNDP</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI) for six consecutive years (2001\xe2\x80\x932006),<sup id="cite_ref-HDI_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and then reclaimed this position in 2009, through 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-HDI2_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI2-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The standard of living in Norway is among the highest in the world. <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a></i> magazine ranks Norway last in its <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Failed_States_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by Failed States Index">Failed States Index</a> for 2009, judging Norway to be the world\'s most well-functioning and stable country. The <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> ranks Norway fourth in the 2013 equalised <a href="/wiki/OECD_Better_Life_Index" title="OECD Better Life Index">Better Life Index</a> and third in intergenerational earnings elasticity.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OECD_Better_Life_Index_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_Better_Life_Index-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg/300px-Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg.png" width="300" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg/450px-Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg/600px-Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Territorial_waters_-_Norway.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Norway\'s claimed economic zones</div></div></div>\n<p>The Norwegian economy is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a>, a prosperous capitalist <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> and social democracy country featuring a combination of <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> activity and large state ownership in certain key sectors. <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Norway" title="Healthcare in Norway">Public health care in Norway</a> is free (after an annual charge of around 2000 <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_krone" title="Norwegian krone">kroner</a> for those over 16), and parents have 46 weeks paid<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> parental leave. The state income derived from natural resources includes a significant contribution from petroleum production. Norway has an unemployment rate of 4.8%, with 68% of the population aged 15\xe2\x80\x9374 employed.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> People in the labour force are either employed or looking for work.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> 9.5% of the population aged 18\xe2\x80\x9366 receive a disability pension<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and 30% of the labour force are employed by the government, the highest in the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The hourly productivity levels, as well as average hourly wages in Norway, are among the highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarian</a> values of Norwegian society have kept the wage difference between the lowest paid worker and the CEO of most companies as much less than in comparable western economies.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> This is also evident in <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_income_equality" title="List of countries by income equality">Norway\'s low Gini coefficient</a>.\n</p><p>The state has large ownership positions in key industrial sectors, such as the strategic petroleum sector (<a href="/wiki/Statoil" class="mw-redirect" title="Statoil">Statoil</a>), hydroelectric energy production (<a href="/wiki/Statkraft" title="Statkraft">Statkraft</a>), aluminium production (<a href="/wiki/Norsk_Hydro" title="Norsk Hydro">Norsk Hydro</a>), the largest Norwegian bank (<a href="/wiki/DNB_ASA" title="DNB ASA">DNB</a>), and telecommunication provider (<a href="/wiki/Telenor" title="Telenor">Telenor</a>). Through these big companies, the government controls approximately 30% of the stock values at the Oslo Stock Exchange. When non-listed companies are included, the state has even higher share in ownership (mainly from direct oil licence ownership). Norway is a major shipping nation and has the world\'s 6th largest <a href="/wiki/Ship_transport" class="mw-redirect" title="Ship transport">merchant fleet</a>, with 1,412 Norwegian-owned merchant vessels.\n</p><p>By referendums in 1972 and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_European_Union_membership_referendum,_1994" title="Norwegian European Union membership referendum, 1994">1994</a>, Norwegians rejected proposals to join the European Union (EU). However, Norway, together with <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, participates in the European Union\'s single market through the European Economic Area (EEA) agreement. The EEA Treaty between the European Union countries and the EFTA countries\xe2\x80\x93 transposed into Norwegian law via "E\xc3\x98S-loven"<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x93 describes the procedures for implementing European Union rules in Norway and the other EFTA countries. Norway is a highly integrated member of most sectors of the EU internal market. Some sectors, such as agriculture, oil and fish, are not wholly covered by the EEA Treaty. Norway has also acceded to the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Agreement" title="Schengen Agreement">Schengen Agreement</a> and several other intergovernmental agreements among the EU member states.\n</p><p>The country is richly endowed with natural resources including petroleum, <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>, fish, <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forests</a>, and minerals. Large reserves of petroleum and natural gas were discovered in the 1960s, which led to a boom in the economy. Norway has obtained one of the highest standards of living in the world in part by having a large amount of natural resources compared to the size of the population. In 2011, 28% of state revenues were generated from the petroleum industry.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway is the first country which banned cutting of trees (deforestation), in order to prevent rain forests from vanishing. The country declared its intention at the UN Climate Summit in 2014, alongside Great Britain and Germany. Crops, that are typically linked to forests\' destruction are timber, soy, palm oil and beef. Now Norway has to find new way to provide these essential products without exerting negative influence on its environment.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources">Resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG/220px-Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG/330px-Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG/440px-Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredvang_Lofoten_2009_2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Agriculture is a significant sector, in spite of the mountainous landscape (<a href="/wiki/Flakstad" title="Flakstad">Flakstad</a>)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg/220px-Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg/330px-Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg/440px-Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stockfish_in_Lofoten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stockfish" title="Stockfish">Stockfish</a> has been exported from <a href="/wiki/Lofoten" title="Lofoten">Lofoten</a> in Norway for at least 1,000 years</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Fish industry</dt></dl>\n<p>Norway is also the world\'s second-largest exporter of fish (in value, after China).<sup id="cite_ref-GGT_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GGT-168">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Fish from fish farms and catch constitutes the second largest (behind oil/natural gas) export product measured in value.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Electricity</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectric plants</a> generate roughly 98\xe2\x80\x9399% of Norway\'s electric power, more than any other country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Mineral resources</dt></dl>\n<p>Norway contains significant mineral resources, and in 2013, its mineral production was valued at US$1.5&#160;billion (Norwegian Geological Survey data). The most valuable minerals are calcium carbonate (<a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>), building stone, <a href="/wiki/Nepheline_syenite" title="Nepheline syenite">nepheline syenite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, iron, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Oil industry</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heidrun,_Norskehavet_(28276260560).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg/220px-Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg/330px-Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg/440px-Heidrun%2C_Norskehavet_%2828276260560%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5460" data-file-height="3630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heidrun,_Norskehavet_(28276260560).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil production has been central to the Norwegian economy since the 1970s, with a dominating <a href="/wiki/State_ownership" title="State ownership">state ownership</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heidrun_oil_field" title="Heidrun oil field">Heidrun oil field</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>Export revenues from oil and gas have risen to almost 50% of total exports and constitute more than 20% of the GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Norway is the fifth-largest oil exporter and third-largest gas exporter in the world, but it is not a member of <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>. In 1995, the Norwegian government established the sovereign wealth fund (<a href="/wiki/The_Government_Pension_Fund_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="The Government Pension Fund of Norway">"Government Pension Fund \xe2\x80\x93 Global"</a>), which would be funded with oil revenues, including taxes, dividends, sales revenues and licensing fees. This was intended to reduce overheating in the economy from oil revenues, minimise uncertainty from volatility in oil price, and provide a cushion to compensate for expenses associated with the ageing of the population.\n</p><p>The government controls its petroleum resources through a combination of state ownership in major operators in the oil fields (with approximately 62% ownership in <a href="/wiki/Statoil" class="mw-redirect" title="Statoil">Statoil</a> in 2007) and the fully state-owned <a href="/wiki/Petoro" title="Petoro">Petoro</a>, which has a market value of about twice Statoil, and <a href="/wiki/State%27s_Direct_Financial_Interest" title="State&#39;s Direct Financial Interest">SDFI</a>. Finally, the government controls licensing of exploration and production of fields. The fund invests in developed financial markets outside Norway. The budgetary rule (<i>Handlingsregelen</i>) is to spend no more than 4% of the fund each year (assumed to be the normal yield from the fund).\n</p><p>In March 2017, the Government Pension Fund controlled assets were valued at approximately US$913&#160;billion (equal to US$182,000 per capita), which is about 178% of Norway\'s current GDP. It is the largest <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_wealth_fund" title="Sovereign wealth fund">sovereign wealth fund</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The fund controls about 1.3% of all listed shares in Europe, and more than 1% of all the publicly traded shares in the world. The Norwegian Central Bank operates investment offices in London, New York, and Shanghai. Guidelines implemented in 2007 allow the fund to invest up to 60% of the capital in shares (maximum of 40% prior), while the rest may be placed in bonds and real-estate. As the stock markets tumbled in September 2008, the fund was able to buy more shares at low prices. In this way, the losses incurred by the market turmoil was recuperated by November 2009.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Other nations with economies based on natural resources, such as Russia, are trying to learn from Norway by establishing similar funds. The investment choices of the Norwegian fund are directed by <a href="/wiki/Socially_responsible_investing" title="Socially responsible investing">ethical guidelines</a>; for example, the fund is not allowed to invest in companies that produce parts for nuclear weapons. Norway\'s highly <a href="/wiki/Transparency_(market)" title="Transparency (market)">transparent</a> investment scheme is lauded by the international community.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The future size of the fund is closely linked to the price of oil and to developments in international financial markets.\n</p><p>In 2000, the government sold one-third of the state-owned oil company Statoil in an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">IPO</a>. The next year, the main telecom supplier, <a href="/wiki/Telenor" title="Telenor">Telenor</a>, was listed on <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Stock_Exchange" title="Oslo Stock Exchange">Oslo Stock Exchange</a>. The state also owns significant shares of Norway\'s largest bank, <a href="/wiki/DNB_ASA" title="DNB ASA">DnB NOR</a> and the airline <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">SAS</a>. Since 2000, economic growth has been rapid, pushing unemployment down to levels not seen since the early 1980s (unemployment in 2007: 1.3%). The international financial crisis has primarily affected the industrial sector, but unemployment has remained low, and was at 3.3% (86,000 people) in August 2011. In contrast to Norway, Sweden had substantially higher actual and projected unemployment numbers as a result of the recession. Thousands of mainly young Swedes migrated to Norway for work during these years, which is easy, as the labour market and social security systems overlap in the Nordic Countries. In the first quarter of 2009, the GNP of Norway surpassed Sweden\'s for the first time in history, although its population is half the size.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Oil fields</dt></dl>\n<p>Between 1966 and 2013, Norwegian companies drilled 5085 oil wells, mostly in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Of these 3672 are <i>utviklingsbr\xc3\xb8nner</i> (regular production);<sup id="cite_ref-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> 1413 are <i>letebr\xc3\xb8nner</i> (exploration); and 1405 have been terminated (<i>avsluttet</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwegianSectorOnly?4053wells-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Oil fields not yet in production phase include: <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wisting_Central&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wisting Central (page does not exist)">Wisting Central</a>\xe2\x80\x94calculated size in 2013, 65\xe2\x80\x93156&#160;million barrels of oil and 10 to 40&#160;billion cubic feet (0.28 to 1.13&#160;billion cubic metres), (<i>utvinnbar</i>) of gas.<sup id="cite_ref-WistingCentral_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WistingCentral-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Castberg_Oil_Field&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Castberg Oil Field (page does not exist)">Castberg Oil Field</a> (<i>Castberg-feltet</i><sup id="cite_ref-WistingCentral_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WistingCentral-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>)\xe2\x80\x94calculated size 540 million barrels of oil, and 2 to 7&#160;billion cubic feet (57 to 198&#160;million cubic metres) (<i>utvinnbar</i>) of gas.<sup id="cite_ref-Castberg-feltet_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castberg-feltet-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Both oil fields are located in the <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Norway" title="Transport in Norway">Transport in Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Norway" title="Rail transport in Norway">Rail transport in Norway</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Norway" title="List of airports in Norway">List of airports in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg/220px-E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg/330px-E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg/440px-E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:E6_Brattlikollen_oktober_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/European_route_E6" title="European route E6">E6</a> highway at Brattlikollen outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the low population density, narrow shape and long coastlines of Norway, its public transport is less developed than in many European countries, especially outside the major cities. The country has long-standing water transport traditions, but the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Ministry_of_Transport_and_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Ministry of Transport and Communications">Norwegian Ministry of Transport and Communications</a> has in recent years implemented rail, road, and air transport through numerous subsidiaries to develop the country\'s infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Under discussion is development of a new high-speed rail system between the nation\'s largest cities.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway\'s main railway network consists of 4,114 kilometres (2,556&#160;mi) of <a href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a> lines, of which 242 kilometres (150&#160;mi) is <a href="/wiki/Double_track" class="mw-redirect" title="Double track">double track</a> and 64 kilometres (40&#160;mi) <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> (210&#160;km/h) while 62% is electrified at <a href="/wiki/15_kV_AC_railway_electrification" title="15 kV AC railway electrification">15&#160;kV &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">16<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>2</sup>&#8260;<sub>3</sub></span>&#160;Hz AC</a>. The railways transported 56,827,000 passengers 2,956 million <a href="/wiki/Units_of_transportation_measurement" title="Units of transportation measurement">passenger-kilometres</a> and 24,783,000 tonnes of cargo 3,414 million <a href="/wiki/Units_of_transportation_measurement" title="Units of transportation measurement">tonne-kilometres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The entire network is owned by the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_National_Rail_Administration" title="Norwegian National Rail Administration">Norwegian National Rail Administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jbvabout_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbvabout-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> All domestic passenger trains except the <a href="/wiki/Flytoget" title="Flytoget">Airport Express Train</a> are operated by <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_State_Railways" title="Norwegian State Railways">Norges Statsbaner</a> (NSB).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Several companies operate freight trains.<sup id="cite_ref-administration1_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-administration1-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\nInvestment in new infrastructure and maintenance is financed through the state budget,<sup id="cite_ref-jbvabout_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbvabout-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> and subsidies are provided for passenger train operations.<sup id="cite_ref-minpt_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minpt-186">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> NSB operates long-haul trains, including <a href="/wiki/NSB_Night_Train" title="NSB Night Train">night trains</a>, regional services and four <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">commuter train</a> systems, around <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Commuter_Rail" title="Oslo Commuter Rail">Oslo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%B8ndelag_Commuter_Rail" title="Tr\xc3\xb8ndelag Commuter Rail">Trondheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bergen_Commuter_Rail" title="Bergen Commuter Rail">Bergen</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A6ren_Line" title="J\xc3\xa6ren Line">Stavanger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg/220px-Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg/330px-Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg/440px-Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4961" data-file-height="2481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oslo_Lufthavn_2017_-_visualisering_luftperspektiv_dag.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Visualization of <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Airport,_Gardermoen" title="Oslo Airport, Gardermoen">Oslo Airport, Gardermoen</a> as of 2017 after the latest expansion and renovation</div></div></div>\n<p>Norway has approximately 92,946 kilometres (57,754&#160;mi) of road network, of which 72,033 kilometres (44,759&#160;mi) are paved and 664 kilometres (413&#160;mi) are motorway.<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The four tiers of road routes are national, county, municipal and private, with national and primary county roads numbered en route. The most important national routes are part of the <a href="/wiki/International_E-road_network" title="International E-road network">European route</a> scheme. The two most prominent are the <a href="/wiki/European_route_E06" class="mw-redirect" title="European route E06">E6</a> going north-south through the entire country, and the <a href="/wiki/European_route_E39" title="European route E39">E39</a>, which follows the West Coast. National and county roads are managed by the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Public_Roads_Administration" title="Norwegian Public Roads Administration">Norwegian Public Roads Administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway has the world\'s largest registered stock of <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicles_in_Norway" title="Plug-in electric vehicles in Norway">plug-in electric vehicles per capita</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NorwayLargest_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayLargest-189">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AVERE_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AVERE-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorwaySales2011_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwaySales2011-191">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In March 2014, Norway became the first country where over 1 in every 100 passenger cars on the roads is a plug-in electric.<sup id="cite_ref-NorwayEVSales032014_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayEVSales032014-192">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The plug-in electric segment <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> of new car sales is also the highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Top6Global2013_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top6Global2013-193">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> According to a report by <a href="/wiki/Dagens_N%C3%A6ringsliv" title="Dagens N\xc3\xa6ringsliv">Dagens N\xc3\xa6ringsliv</a> in June 2016, the country would like to ban all gasoline and diesel powered vehicles as early as 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> In June 2017, 42% of new cars registered were electric.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of the 98 airports in Norway,<sup id="cite_ref-factbook_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-factbook-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> 52 are public,<sup id="cite_ref-avinorpassengers_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avinorpassengers-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> and 46 are operated by the state-owned <a href="/wiki/Avinor" title="Avinor">Avinor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_airports_in_the_Nordic_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="List of the largest airports in the Nordic countries">Seven airports</a> have more than one million passengers annually.<sup id="cite_ref-avinorpassengers_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avinorpassengers-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> A total of 41,089,675 passengers passed through Norwegian airports in 2007, of whom 13,397,458 were international.<sup id="cite_ref-avinorpassengers_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avinorpassengers-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The central gateway to Norway by air is <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Airport,_Gardermoen" title="Oslo Airport, Gardermoen">Oslo Airport, Gardermoen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-avinorpassengers_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-avinorpassengers-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Located about 35 kilometres (22&#160;mi) northeast of Oslo, it is <a href="/wiki/Airline_hub" title="Airline hub">hub</a> for the two major Norwegian airlines: <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">Scandinavian Airlines</a><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Air_Shuttle" title="Norwegian Air Shuttle">Norwegian Air Shuttle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> and for regional aircraft from Western Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-wideroemap_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wideroemap-200">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> There are departures to most European countries and some intercontinental destinations.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> A direct high-speed train connects to Oslo Central Station every 10 minutes for a 20 min ride.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Norway" title="Demographics of Norway">Demographics of Norway</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Norway" title="Languages of Norway">Languages of Norway</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">Sami languages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Norwegian_dialects.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Norwegian_dialects.PNG/220px-Norwegian_dialects.PNG" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Norwegian_dialects.PNG/330px-Norwegian_dialects.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Norwegian_dialects.PNG/440px-Norwegian_dialects.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1405" data-file-height="1856" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Norwegian_dialects.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of Norwegian dialect groups: North Norwegian (yellow), Tr\xc3\xb8ndelag Norwegian (navy blue), West Norwegian (orange) and East Norwegian (pale blue).</div></div></div>\n<p>Norwegian and Sami are the two official languages of Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic</a> Norwegian language has two official written forms, <i><a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokm\xc3\xa5l">Bokm\xc3\xa5l</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a></i>. Both are used in public administration, schools, churches, and media. Bokm\xc3\xa5l is the written language used by a large majority of about 80\xe2\x80\x9385%. Around 95% of the population speak Norwegian as their first or native language, although many speak   dialects that may differ significantly from the written languages. All Norwegian dialects are mutually intelligible, although listeners with limited exposure to dialects other than their own may struggle to understand certain phrases and pronunciations in some other dialects.\n</p><p>Several <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a> Sami languages are spoken and written throughout the country, especially in the north, by some members of the Sami people. (Estimates suggest that about one third of the Norwegian Sami speak a Sami language.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>) Speakers have a right to be educated and to receive communication from the government in their own language in a special <i>forvaltningsomr\xc3\xa5de</i> (administrative area) for Sami languages.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kven_people" title="Kven people">Kven</a> minority historically spoke the Uralic <a href="/wiki/Kven_language" title="Kven language">Kven language</a> (considered a separate language in Norway, but generally perceived as a Finnish dialect in Finland). Today the majority of ethnic Kven have little or no knowledge of the language. According to the <a href="/wiki/Kainun_institutti" title="Kainun institutti">Kainun institutti</a>, "The typical modern Kven is a Norwegian-speaking Norwegian who knows his genealogy."<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> As Norway has ratified the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> (ECRML) the Kven language together with <a href="/wiki/Romani_language" title="Romani language">Romani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandoromani_language" title="Scandoromani language">Scandoromani language</a> has become officially recognised minority languages.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some supporters have also advocated making <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sign_Language" title="Norwegian Sign Language">Norwegian Sign Language</a> an official language of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 19th and 20th centuries, the Norwegian language was subject to <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language_conflict" title="Norwegian language conflict">strong political and cultural controversies</a>. This led to the development of Nynorsk in the 19th century and to the formation of alternative spelling standards in the 20th century.\n</p><p>Norwegian is similar to the other languages in Scandinavia: <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>. All three languages are to a degree mutually intelligible and can be, and commonly are, employed in communication among inhabitants of the Scandinavian countries. As a result of the co-operation within the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>, inhabitants of all Nordic countries, including <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, have the right to communicate with Norwegian authorities in their own language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Students who are children of immigrant parents are encouraged to learn the Norwegian language. The Norwegian government offers language instructional courses for immigrants wishing to obtain Norwegian citizenship. With increasing concern about assimilating immigrants, since 1 September 2008, the government has required that an applicant for Norwegian citizenship give evidence of proficiency in either Norwegian or in one of the Sami languages, or give proof of having attended classes in Norwegian for 300 hours, or meet the language requirements for university studies in Norway (that is, by being proficient in one of the Scandinavian languages).\n</p><p>The primary foreign language taught in Norwegian schools is English, considered an international language since the post-WWII era. The majority of the population is fairly fluent in English, especially those born after World War II. German, French and Spanish are also commonly taught as second or, more often, third languages. Russian, Japanese, Italian, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and rarely <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Chinese (Mandarin)</a> are offered in some schools, mostly in the cities. Traditionally, English, German and French were considered the main foreign languages in Norway. These languages, for instance, were used on <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_passport" title="Norwegian passport">Norwegian passports</a> until the 1990s, and university students have a general right to use these languages when submitting their theses.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="toccolours" style="width:15em;border-spacing: 0;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.5em 0 1em 0.5em;"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-title" colspan="3" style="padding:0.25em;font-size:110%">Historical population</th></tr><tr style="font-size:95%"><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px;width:3em">Year</th><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px 2px;text-align:right"><abbr style="text-align:center;padding:1px">1735</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">616,109</td><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">+0.48%</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">2013</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">5,096,300</td><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">+1.61%</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">2060<br /><span style="font-size:85%;">(projected)</span></th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">7,032,687</td><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">+0.69%</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="border-top:1px solid black;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><b>Source</b>: Statistics Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Norway\'s population was 5,096,300 people in October 2013. <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a> are an ethnic North <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> people. Since the late 20th century, Norway has attracted immigrants from southern and central Europe, the Mideast, Africa, Asia and beyond.\n</p><p>In 2012, an official study showed that 86%<sup id="cite_ref-ssb.no_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssb.no-216">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> of the total population have at least one parent who was born in Norway. More than 710,000 individuals (13%)<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> are immigrants and their descendants; there are 117,000 children of immigrants, born in Norway.\n</p><p>Of these 710,000 immigrants and their descendants:\n</p>\n<ul><li>323,000 (39%)<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> have a <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western</a> background (Australia, North America, elsewhere in Europe)</li>\n<li>505,000 (61%)<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> have a non-Western background (primarily <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Moroccan people">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi people">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Kurdistan federal region</a>, Pakistan and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian people">Iran</a> including <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Province" title="Kurdistan Province">Kurdistan Province</a>).</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A6r%C3%B8ydalen_-_no-nb_digifoto_20150121_00098_NB_MIT_FNR_19592.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/N%C3%A6r%C3%B8ydalen_-_no-nb_digifoto_20150121_00098_NB_MIT_FNR_19592.jpg/440px-N%C3%A6r%C3%B8ydalen_-_no-nb_digifoto_20150121_00098_NB_MIT_FNR_19592.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5006" data-file-height="6339" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A6r%C3%B8ydalen_-_no-nb_digifoto_20150121_00098_NB_MIT_FNR_19592.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Norwegian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian people">Ethnic</a> woman in Norwegian local geographic environment at <a href="/wiki/Voss" title="Voss">Voss</a> near <a href="/wiki/Gudvangen" title="Gudvangen">Gudvangen</a> in 1959</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG/300px-Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG" width="300" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG/450px-Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG/600px-Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG 2x" data-file-width="974" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basic_demographics_of_Norway_1900_2000.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demographics in Norway<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2018)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 2013, the Norwegian government said that 14% of the Norwegian population were immigrants or children of two immigrant parents. About 6% of the immigrant population come from EU, North America and Australia, and about 8.1% come from Asia, Africa and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-ssb13_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssb13-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, of the total 660,000 with immigrant background, 407,262 had Norwegian citizenship (62.2%).<sup id="cite_ref-SN2_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SN2-219">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Immigrants have settled in all <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian people">Norwegian</a> municipalities. The cities or municipalities with the highest share of immigrants in 2012 were <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a> (32%) and <a href="/wiki/Drammen" title="Drammen">Drammen</a> (27%).<sup id="cite_ref-innvbef_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-innvbef-220">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The share in Stavanger was 16%.<sup id="cite_ref-innvbef_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-innvbef-220">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a>, Oslo is the "fastest growing city in Europe because of increased immigration".<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, immigration has accounted for most of Norway\'s population growth. In 2011, 16% of newborn children were of immigrant background.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami people</a> are indigenous to the Far North and have traditionally inhabited central and northern parts of Norway and Sweden, as well as areas in northern Finland and in Russia on the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>. Another national minority are the <a href="/wiki/Kven_people" title="Kven people">Kven people</a>, descendants of Finnish-speaking people who migrated to northern Norway from the 18th up to the 20th century. From the 19th century up to the 1970s, the Norwegian government tried to assimilate both the Sami and the Kven, encouraging them to adopt the majority language, culture and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Because of this "<a href="/wiki/Norwegianization" title="Norwegianization">Norwegianization</a> process", many families of Sami or Kven ancestry now identify as ethnic Norwegian.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Emigration</dt></dl>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_diaspora" title="Norwegian diaspora">Norwegian diaspora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Americans" title="Norwegian Americans">Norwegian Americans</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stpaul001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stpaul001.jpg/220px-Stpaul001.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stpaul001.jpg/330px-Stpaul001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stpaul001.jpg/440px-Stpaul001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="590" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stpaul001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Minneapolis%E2%80%93Saint_Paul" title="Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x93Saint Paul">Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x93Saint Paul</a> has the largest concentration of ethnic Norwegians outside Norway, at 470,000.</div></div></div>\n<p>Particularly in the 19th century, when economic conditions were difficult in Norway, tens of thousands of people migrated to the United States and Canada, where they could work and buy land in frontier areas. Many went to the Midwest and Pacific Northwest. In 2006, according to the US Census Bureau, almost 4.7&#160;million persons identified as <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian American">Norwegian Americans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> which was larger than the population of ethnic Norwegians in Norway itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Pop16_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pop16-225">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> In the 2011 Canadian census, 452,705 Canadian citizens identified as having <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Canadian">Norwegian ancestry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwegian_Canadians_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwegian_Canadians-226">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Immigration</dt></dl>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Norway" title="Immigration to Norway">Immigration to Norway</a></div>\n<p>On 1 January 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the number of immigrants or children of two immigrants residing in Norway was 710,465, or 14.1% of the total population,<sup id="cite_ref-ssb13_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssb13-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> up from 183,000 in 1992. Yearly immigration has increased since 2005. While yearly net immigration in 2001\xe2\x80\x932005 was on average 13,613, it increased to 37,541 between 2006 and 2010, and in 2011 net immigration reached 47,032.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> This is mostly because of increased immigration by residents of the EU, in particular from Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, the immigrant community (which includes immigrants and children born in Norway of immigrant parents) grew by 55,300, a record high.<sup id="cite_ref-ssb13_218-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssb13-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Net immigration from abroad reached 47,300 (300 higher than in 2011), while immigration accounted for 72% of Norway\'s population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> 17% of newborn children were born to immigrant parents.<sup id="cite_ref-ssb13_218-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssb13-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Children of Pakistani, Somali and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Vietnamese" title="Norwegian Vietnamese">Vietnamese</a> parents made up the largest groups of all Norwegians born to immigrant parents.<sup id="cite_ref-census_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-230">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable float-right">\n<caption>Largest immigrant groups\n</caption>\n<caption>(1st and 2nd generation):<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-231">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>National background\n</th>\n<th>Population\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Poland\n</td>\n<td>97,196\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Lithuania\n</td>\n<td>37,638\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sweden\n</td>\n<td>36,315\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Somalia\n</td>\n<td>28,696\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Germany\n</td>\n<td>24,601\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Iraq including <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Kurdistan region</a>\n</td>\n<td>22,493\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Syria\n</td>\n<td>20,823\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Philippines\n</td>\n<td>20,537\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pakistan\n</td>\n<td>19,973\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Eritrea\n</td>\n<td>19,957\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Norwegians" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Norwegians">Pakistani Norwegians</a> are the largest non-European minority group in Norway. Most of their 32,700 members live in and around Oslo. The <a href="/wiki/Iraqis_in_Norway" title="Iraqis in Norway">Iraqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a> immigrant populations have increased significantly in recent years. After the enlargement of the EU in 2004, a wave of immigrants arrived from Central and Northern Europe, particularly Poland, Sweden and Lithuania. The fastest growing immigrant groups in 2011 in absolute numbers were from Poland, Lithuania and Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-Ssb.no_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ssb.no-232">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> The policies of immigration and integration have been the subject of much debate in Norway.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-very_long" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_size" title="Wikipedia:Article size">too long</a> to read and navigate comfortably</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please consider <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">splitting</a> content into sub-articles, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">condensing</a> it, or adding or removing <a href="/wiki/Help:Section#Subsections" title="Help:Section">subheadings</a>.</span>  <small><i>(January 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Norway" title="Religion in Norway">Religion in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nidaros_Cathedral,_Trondheim,_West_view_20150605_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg/220px-Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg/330px-Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg/440px-Nidaros_Cathedral%2C_Trondheim%2C_West_view_20150605_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4260" data-file-height="4286" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nidaros_Cathedral,_Trondheim,_West_view_20150605_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nidaros_Cathedral" title="Nidaros Cathedral">Nidaros Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state#Norway" title="Separation of church and state">Separation of church and state</a> happened significantly later in Norway than in most of Europe and is not yet complete. In 2012, the Norwegian parliament voted to grant the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Norway" title="Church of Norway">Church of Norway</a> greater autonomy,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> a decision which was confirmed in a constitutional amendment on 21 May 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until 2012 parliamentary officials were required to be members of the Evangelical-Lutheran Church of Norway, and at least half of all government ministers had to be a member of the state church. As state church, the Church of Norway\'s clergy were viewed as state employees, and the central and regional church administrations were part of the state administration. Members of the Royal family are required to be members of the Lutheran church. On 1 January 2017, Norway made the church independent of the state, but retained the Church\'s status as the "people\'s church".<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stavechurch-heddal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Stavechurch-heddal.jpg/220px-Stavechurch-heddal.jpg" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Stavechurch-heddal.jpg/330px-Stavechurch-heddal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Stavechurch-heddal.jpg/440px-Stavechurch-heddal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3218" data-file-height="2840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stavechurch-heddal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Heddal_stave_church" title="Heddal stave church">Heddal stave church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Notodden" title="Notodden">Notodden</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Stave_church" title="Stave church">stave church</a> in Norway</div></div></div>\n<p>Most Norwegians are registered at baptism as members of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Norway" title="Church of Norway">Church of Norway</a>, which has been Norway\'s <a href="/wiki/State_church" class="mw-redirect" title="State church">state church</a> since its establishment. In recent years the church has been granted increasing internal autonomy, but it retains its special constitutional status and other special ties to the state, and the constitution requires that the reigning monarch must be a member and states that the country\'s values are based on its Christian and humanist heritage. Many remain in the church to participate in the community and practices such as <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a>, marriage and burial rites. About 70.6% of Norwegians were members of the Church of Norway in 2017.
