In 2017, about 53.6% of all newborns were baptised and about 57.9% of all 15-year-old persons were <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Lutheran_Church)" title="Confirmation (Lutheran Church)">confirmed</a> in the church.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 1990s, studies estimated that between 4.7% and 5.3% of Norwegians attended church on a weekly basis.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> This figure has dropped to about 2%.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2010, 10% of the population was <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">religiously unaffiliated</a>, while another 9%, were members of religious communities outside the Church of Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-Norway1_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norway1-241">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Other Christian denominations total about 4.9%<sup id="cite_ref-Norway1_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norway1-241">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> of the population, the largest of which is the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, with 83,000 members, according to 2009 government statistics.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Aftenposten</i> (Norwegian, The Evening Post) in October 2012 reported there were about 115,234 registered Roman Catholics in Norway; the reporter estimated that the total number of people with a Roman Catholic background may be 170,000\xe2\x80\x93200,000 or higher.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Others include <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostals</a> (39,600),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Free_Church_of_Norway" title="Evangelical Lutheran Free Church of Norway">Evangelical Lutheran Free Church of Norway</a> (19,600),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methodists" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodists">Methodists</a> (11,000),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a> (9,900),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> (9,900),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brunstad_Christian_Church" title="Brunstad Christian Church">Brunstad Christian Church</a> (6,800),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventists" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh-day Adventists">Seventh-day Adventists</a> (5,100),<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Iraq#Christianity" title="Religion in Iraq">Assyrians and Chaldeans</a>, and others. The Swedish, Finnish and Icelandic Lutheran congregations in Norway have about 27,500 members in total.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Other Christian denominations comprise less than 1% each, including 4,000 members in <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> and 12,000 <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah&#39;s Witnesses">Jehovah\'s Witnesses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion2_242-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion2-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg/220px-2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg/330px-2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg/440px-2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2755" data-file-height="2065" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-04-08_Bait-un-Nasr_mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Baitun_Nasr_Mosque" title="Baitun Nasr Mosque">Baitun Nasr Mosque</a> on the outskirts of Oslo is the largest mosque in Norway<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Among non-Christian religions, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Norway" title="Islam in Norway">Islam</a> is the largest, with 132,135 registered members (2014), and probably fewer than 200,000 in total.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-245">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> It is practised mainly by <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_diaspora" title="Arab diaspora">Arab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurdish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> immigrants, as well as <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Norwegians" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Norwegians">Norwegians of Pakistani descent</a>.\n</p><p>Other religions comprise less than 1% each, including 819 adherents of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Norway" title="History of the Jews in Norway">Judaism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Indian immigrants introduced <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> to Norway, which in 2011 has slightly more than 5,900 adherents, or 1% of non-Lutheran Norwegians.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> has approximately 3,000 adherents, with most living in Oslo, which has two <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwaras</a>. Sikhs first came to Norway in the early 1970s. The troubles in Punjab after <a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Star" title="Operation Blue Star">Operation Blue Star</a> and riots committed against Sikhs in India after the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Indira_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Indira Gandhi">assassination of Indira Gandhi</a> led to an increase in Sikh refugees moving to Norway. Drammen also has a sizeable population of Sikhs; the largest gurdwara in north Europe was built in <a href="/wiki/Lier,_Norway" title="Lier, Norway">Lier</a>. There are eleven <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> organisations, grouped under the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_Federation_of_Norway" title="Buddhist Federation of Norway">Buddhistforbundet</a> organisation, with slightly over 14,000 members,<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion_246-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> which make up 0.2% of the population. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad Faith">Baha\'i</a> religion has slightly more than 1,000 adherents.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwayreligion_246-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwayreligion-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Around 1.7% (84,500) of Norwegians belong to the secular <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Humanist_Association" title="Norwegian Humanist Association">Norwegian Humanist Association</a>.\n</p><p>From 2006 to 2011, the fastest-growing religious communities in Norway were <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_in_Norway" title="Eastern Orthodoxy in Norway">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy_in_Norway" title="Oriental Orthodoxy in Norway">Oriental Orthodox Christianity</a>, which grew in membership by 80%; however, their share of the total population remains small, at 0.2%. It is associated with the huge immigration from <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, and to a lesser extent from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and Eastern European and Middle Eastern countries. Other fast-growing religions were the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Norway">Roman Catholic Church</a> (78.7%), <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Norway" title="Hinduism in Norway">Hinduism</a> (59.6%), <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Norway" title="Islam in Norway">Islam</a> (48.1%), and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Norway" title="Buddhism in Norway">Buddhism</a> (46.7%).<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As in other Scandinavian countries, the ancient Norse followed a form of native <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Norse_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse paganism">Norse paganism</a>. By the end of the 11th century, when Norway had been <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianization of Scandinavia">Christianised</a>, the indigenous Norse religion and practices were prohibited. Remnants of the native religion and beliefs of Norway survive today in the form of names, referential names of cities and locations, the days of the week, and other parts of everyday language. Modern interest in the old ways has led to a revival of pagan religious practices in the form of <i><a href="/wiki/Germanic_Neopaganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Neopaganism">\xc3\x85satru</a>.</i> The Norwegian <i>\xc3\x85satrufellesskapet Bifrost</i> formed in 1996; in 2011, the fellowship had about 300 members. <i>Foreningen Forn Sed</i> was formed in 1999 and has been recognised by the Norwegian government.\n</p><p>The Sami minority retained their <a href="/wiki/Sami_shamanism" title="Sami shamanism">shamanistic religion</a> well into the 18th century, when most converted to Christianity under the influence of Dano-Norwegian Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Missionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a>. Although some insist that "indigenous Sami religion had effectively been eradicated,\'<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> athropologist <a href="/wiki/Gutorm_Gjessing" title="Gutorm Gjessing">Gutorm Gjessing</a>\'s <i>Changing Lapps</i> (1954) argues that the Samis "were outwardly and to all practical purposes converted to Christianity, but at the subconscious and unconscious level, the shamistic frenzy survived, more or less latent, only awaiting the necessary stimulus to break out into the open."<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Today there is a renewed appreciation for the Sami traditional way of life, which has led to a revival of <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noaidevuohta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Noaidevuohta (page does not exist)">Noaidevuohta</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Some Norwegian and Sami celebrities are reported to visit <a href="/wiki/Shamans" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamans">shamans</a> for guidance.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the 2010 Eurobarometer Poll, 22% of Norwegian citizens responded that "they believe there is a God", 44% responded that "they believe there is some sort of spirit or life force" and 29% responded that "they don\'t believe there is any sort of spirit, God or life force". Five percent gave no response.<sup id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_2010_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer_2010-253">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Norway" title="Health in Norway">Health in Norway</a></div>\n<p>Norway was awarded first place according to the UN\'s <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI) for 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In the 1800s, by contrast, poverty and <a href="/wiki/Communicable_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Communicable disease">communicable diseases</a> dominated in Norway together with <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famines</a> and epidemics. From the 1900s, improvements in public health occurred as a result of development in several areas such as social and <a href="/wiki/Living_condition" class="mw-redirect" title="Living condition">living conditions</a>, changes in disease and medical outbreaks, establishment of the health care system, and emphasis on public health matters. <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> and increased treatment opportunities with antibiotics resulted in great improvements within the Norwegian population. Improved hygiene and better nutrition were factors that contributed to improved health.\n</p><p>The disease pattern in Norway changed from communicable diseases to non-communicable diseases and chronic diseases as <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>. Inequalities and social differences are still present in public health in Norway today.<sup id="cite_ref-Folkehelse_i_Norge_1814_\xe2\x80\x93_2014_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folkehelse_i_Norge_1814_\xe2\x80\x93_2014-255">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013 the infant mortality rate was 2.5 per 1,000 live births among children under the age of one. For girls it was 2.7 and for boys 2.3, which is the lowest infant mortality rate for boys ever recorded in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Norway" title="Education in Norway">Education in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg/220px-NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg/330px-NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg/440px-NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NTNU_Trondheim_Mainbuilding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main building of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_University_of_Science_and_Technology" title="Norwegian University of Science and Technology">Norwegian University of Science and Technology</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Norway" title="Higher education in Norway">Higher education in Norway</a> is offered by a range of seven <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Norway" title="List of universities in Norway">universities</a>, five specialised colleges, 25 <a href="/wiki/University_college" title="University college">university colleges</a> as well as a range of private colleges. Education follows the <a href="/wiki/Bologna_Process" title="Bologna Process">Bologna Process</a> involving <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor&#39;s degree">Bachelor</a> (3 years), <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master&#39;s degree">Master</a> (2 years) and PhD (3 years) degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Acceptance is offered after finishing <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Norway" title="Education in Norway">upper secondary school</a> with general study competence.\n</p><p>Public education is virtually free, regardless of nationality.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> The academic year has two <a href="/wiki/Academic_term" title="Academic term">semesters</a>, from August to December and from January to June. The ultimate responsibility for the education lies with the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Ministry_of_Education_and_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Ministry of Education and Research">Norwegian Ministry of Education and Research</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Norway" title="Culture of Norway">Culture of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bryggen,_Bergen3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/220px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/330px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG/440px-Bryggen%2C_Bergen3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2544" data-file-height="1696" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bryggen,_Bergen3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bryggen" title="Bryggen">Bryggen</a> in Bergen is on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Europe">list of UNESCO World Heritage Sites</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Norwegian <a href="/w/index.php?title=Farm_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Farm culture (page does not exist)">farm culture</a> continues to play a role in contemporary Norwegian culture. In the 19th century, it inspired a strong <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_romantic_nationalism" title="Norwegian romantic nationalism">romantic nationalistic</a> movement, which is still visible in the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Media_in_Norway" title="Category:Media in Norway">media</a>. Norwegian culture blossomed with nationalist efforts to achieve an independent identity in the areas of literature, art and music. This continues today in the performing arts and as a result of government support for exhibitions, cultural projects and artwork.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmine_Seippel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Wilhelmine_Seippel.jpg/220px-Wilhelmine_Seippel.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Wilhelmine_Seippel.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="298" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilhelmine_Seippel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional Norwegian farmer\'s costumes, known as <i>folkedrakt</i>, and modern costumes inspired by those costumes, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bunad" title="Bunad">bunad</a></i>, are widely used on special occasions.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Norway has been considered a progressive country, which has adopted legislation and policies to support women\'s rights, minority rights, and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Norway" title="LGBT rights in Norway">LGBT rights</a>. As early as 1884, 171 of the leading figures, among them five Prime Ministers for the Liberal Party and the Conservative Party, co-founded the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Association_for_Women%27s_Rights" title="Norwegian Association for Women&#39;s Rights">Norwegian Association for Women\'s Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> They successfully campaigned for women\'s <a href="/wiki/Right_to_education" title="Right to education">right to education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women\'s suffrage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_work" title="Right to work">right to work</a>, and other gender equality policies. From the 1970s, gender equality also came high on the state agenda, with the establishment of a public body to promote gender equality, which evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Gender_Equality_and_Anti-Discrimination_Ombud" title="Gender Equality and Anti-Discrimination Ombud">Gender Equality and Anti-Discrimination Ombud</a>. Civil society organisations also continue to play an important role, and the women\'s rights organisations are today organised in the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Women%27s_Lobby" title="Norwegian Women&#39;s Lobby">Norwegian Women\'s Lobby</a> umbrella organisation.\n</p><p>In 1990, the Norwegian constitution was amended to grant <a href="/wiki/Absolute_primogeniture" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute primogeniture">absolute primogeniture</a> to the Norwegian throne, meaning that the eldest child, regardless of gender, takes precedence in the line of succession. As it was not retroactive, the current successor to the throne is the eldest son of the King, rather than his eldest child. The Norwegian constitution Article 6 states that "For those born before the year 1990 it shall...be the case that a male shall take precedence over a female."<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sami people have for centuries been the subject of discrimination and abuse by the dominant cultures in Scandinavia and Russia, those countries claiming possession of Sami lands.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The Sami people have never been a single community in a single region of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1pmi" title="S\xc3\xa1pmi">Lapland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Norway has been greatly criticised by the international community for the politics of <a href="/wiki/Norwegianization" title="Norwegianization">Norwegianization</a> of and discrimination against the indigenous population of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Norway was, in 1990, the first country to recognise <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_and_Tribal_Peoples_Convention,_1989" title="Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention, 1989">ILO-convention 169</a> on <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Indigenous people">indigenous people</a> recommended by the UN.\n</p><p>In regard to LGBT rights, Norway was the first country in the world to enact an anti-discrimination law protecting the rights of gays and lesbians. In 1993, Norway became the second country to legalise <a href="/wiki/Civil_union" title="Civil union">civil union</a> partnerships for same-sex couples, and on 1 January 2009 <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Norway" title="Same-sex marriage in Norway">Norway became the sixth country</a> to grant full <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">marriage equality</a> to same-sex couples. As a promoter of human rights, Norway has held the annual <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Freedom_Forum" title="Oslo Freedom Forum">Oslo Freedom Forum</a> conference, a gathering described by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> as "on its way to becoming a human-rights equivalent of the Davos economic forum."<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Norway" title="Cinema of Norway">Cinema of Norway</a></div>\n<p>The Norwegian cinema has received international recognition. The documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Kon-Tiki_(1950_film)" title="Kon-Tiki (1950 film)">Kon-Tiki</a></i> (1950) won an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a>. In 1959, <a href="/wiki/Arne_Skouen" title="Arne Skouen">Arne Skouen</a>\'s <i>Nine Lives</i> was nominated, but failed to win. Another notable film is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pinchcliffe_Grand_Prix" title="The Pinchcliffe Grand Prix">Fl\xc3\xa5klypa Grand Prix</a></i> (English: <i>Pinchcliffe Grand Prix</i>), an animated feature film directed by <a href="/wiki/Ivo_Caprino" title="Ivo Caprino">Ivo Caprino</a>. The film was released in 1975 and is based on characters from Norwegian cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Kjell_Aukrust" title="Kjell Aukrust">Kjell Aukrust</a>. It is the most widely seen Norwegian film of all time.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nils_Gaup" title="Nils Gaup">Nils Gaup</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pathfinder_(1987_film)" title="Pathfinder (1987 film)">Pathfinder</a></i> (1987), the story of the <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami</a>, was nominated for an Oscar. <a href="/wiki/Berit_Nesheim" title="Berit Nesheim">Berit Nesheim</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Other_Side_of_Sunday" title="The Other Side of Sunday">The Other Side of Sunday</a></i> was nominated for an Oscar in 1997.\n</p><p>Since the 1990s, the film industry has thrived, producing up to 20 feature films each year. Particular successes were <i><a href="/wiki/Kristin_Lavransdatter" title="Kristin Lavransdatter">Kristin Lavransdatter</a></i>, based on a novel by a Nobel Prize winner; <i><a href="/wiki/The_Telegraphist" title="The Telegraphist">The Telegraphist</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gurin_with_the_Foxtail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gurin with the Foxtail (page does not exist)">Gurin with the Foxtail</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Knut_Erik_Jensen" title="Knut Erik Jensen">Knut Erik Jensen</a> was among the more successful new directors, together with <a href="/wiki/Erik_Skjoldbj%C3%A6rg" title="Erik Skjoldbj\xc3\xa6rg">Erik Skjoldbj\xc3\xa6rg</a>, who is remembered for <i><a href="/wiki/Insomnia_(1997_film)" title="Insomnia (1997 film)">Insomnia</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The country has also been used as filming location for several Hollywood and other international productions, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Empire_Strikes_Back" title="The Empire Strikes Back">The Empire Strikes Back</a></i> (1980), for which the producers used <a href="/wiki/Hardangerj%C3%B8kulen" title="Hardangerj\xc3\xb8kulen">Hardangerj\xc3\xb8kulen</a> <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glacier</a> as a filming location for scenes of the ice planet Hoth. It included a memorable battle in the snow. The films <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Another_Day" title="Die Another Day">Die Another Day</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Compass_(film)" title="The Golden Compass (film)">The Golden Compass</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spies_Like_Us" title="Spies Like Us">Spies Like Us</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Heroes_of_Telemark" title="The Heroes of Telemark">Heroes of Telemark</a>,</i> as well as the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Lilyhammer" title="Lilyhammer">Lilyhammer</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vikings_(2013_TV_series)" title="Vikings (2013 TV series)">Vikings</a></i> also had scenes set in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> A short film, <i>The Spirit of Norway</i> was featured at <a href="/wiki/Maelstrom_(ride)" title="Maelstrom (ride)">Maelstrom</a> at <a href="/wiki/Norway_(Epcot)" class="mw-redirect" title="Norway (Epcot)">Norway</a> Pavilion at <a href="/wiki/Epcot" title="Epcot">Epcot</a> located within <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World_Resort" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney World Resort">Walt Disney World Resort</a> in Florida in the United States. The attraction and the film ceased their operations on 5 October 2014.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Norway" title="Music of Norway">Music of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edvard_Grieg_(1888)_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg/170px-Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg/255px-Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg/340px-Edvard_Grieg_%281888%29_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="863" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edvard_Grieg_(1888)_by_Elliot_and_Fry_-_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Edvard Grieg</a>, composer and pianist</div></div></div><p> The classical <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Norway" title="Music of Norway">music</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic</a> composers <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Edvard Grieg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rikard_Nordraak" title="Rikard Nordraak">Rikard Nordraak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johan_Svendsen" title="Johan Svendsen">Johan Svendsen</a> is internationally known, as is the modern music of <a href="/wiki/Arne_Nordheim" title="Arne Nordheim">Arne Nordheim</a>. Norway\'s classical performers include <a href="/wiki/Leif_Ove_Andsnes" title="Leif Ove Andsnes">Leif Ove Andsnes</a>, one of the world\'s more famous pianists; <a href="/wiki/Truls_M%C3%B8rk" title="Truls M\xc3\xb8rk">Truls M\xc3\xb8rk</a>, an outstanding cellist; and the great Wagnerian soprano <a href="/wiki/Kirsten_Flagstad" title="Kirsten Flagstad">Kirsten Flagstad</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Early_Norwegian_black_metal_scene" title="Early Norwegian black metal scene">Norwegian black metal</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Rock_music_in_Norway" title="Rock music in Norway">rock music in Norway</a>, has been an influence in world music since the late 20th century. Since the 1990s, Norway\'s export of <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">black metal</a>, a lo-fi, dark and raw form of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a>, has been developed by such bands as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_(band)" title="Emperor (band)">Emperor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darkthrone" title="Darkthrone">Darkthrone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorgoroth" title="Gorgoroth">Gorgoroth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">Mayhem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burzum" title="Burzum">Burzum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immortal_(band)" title="Immortal (band)">Immortal</a>. More recently bands such as <a href="/wiki/Enslaved_(band)" title="Enslaved (band)">Enslaved</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kvelertak" title="Kvelertak">Kvelertak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimmu_Borgir" title="Dimmu Borgir">Dimmu Borgir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Satyricon" title="Satyricon">Satyricon</a> have evolved the genre into the present day while still garnering worldwide fans. Controversial events associated with the black metal movement in the early 1990s included several <a href="/wiki/Church_arson" title="Church arson">church burnings</a> and two prominent <a href="/wiki/Black_metal#Aarseth&#39;s_murder" title="Black metal">murder cases</a>.\n</p><p>The jazz scene in Norway is thriving. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Garbarek" title="Jan Garbarek">Jan Garbarek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terje_Rypdal" title="Terje Rypdal">Terje Rypdal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mari_Boine" title="Mari Boine">Mari Boine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arild_Andersen" title="Arild Andersen">Arild Andersen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bugge_Wesseltoft" title="Bugge Wesseltoft">Bugge Wesseltoft</a> are internationally recognised while <a href="/wiki/Paal_Nilssen-Love" title="Paal Nilssen-Love">Paal Nilssen-Love</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supersilent" title="Supersilent">Supersilent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaga_Jazzist" title="Jaga Jazzist">Jaga Jazzist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wibutee" title="Wibutee">Wibutee</a> are becoming world-class artists of the younger generation.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FeleHel_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FeleHel_%282%29.jpg/170px-FeleHel_%282%29.jpg" width="170" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FeleHel_%282%29.jpg/255px-FeleHel_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FeleHel_%282%29.jpg/340px-FeleHel_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="802" data-file-height="1571" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FeleHel_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hardanger_fiddle" title="Hardanger fiddle">Hardingfele</a>, the "Hardanger fiddle", a Norwegian instrument</div></div></div><p> Norway has a strong <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a> tradition which remains popular to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Among the most prominent folk musicians are <a href="/wiki/Hardanger_fiddle" title="Hardanger fiddle">Hardanger fiddlers</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Een" title="Andrea Een">Andrea Een</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olav_J%C3%B8rgen_Hegge" title="Olav J\xc3\xb8rgen Hegge">Olav J\xc3\xb8rgen Hegge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Annbj%C3%B8rg_Lien" title="Annbj\xc3\xb8rg Lien">Annbj\xc3\xb8rg Lien</a>, and the vocalists <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Buen_Garn%C3%A5s" title="Agnes Buen Garn\xc3\xa5s">Agnes Buen Garn\xc3\xa5s</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirsten_Br%C3%A5ten_Berg" title="Kirsten Br\xc3\xa5ten Berg">Kirsten Br\xc3\xa5ten Berg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odd_Nordstoga" title="Odd Nordstoga">Odd Nordstoga</a>.\n</p><p>Other internationally recognised bands are <a href="/wiki/A-ha" title="A-ha">A-ha</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6yksopp" title="R\xc3\xb6yksopp">R\xc3\xb6yksopp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ylvis" title="Ylvis">Ylvis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> A-ha initially rose to global fame during the mid-1980s. In the 1990s and 2000s, the group maintained its popularity domestically, and has remained successful outside Norway, especially in Germany, Switzerland, France, and Brazil.\n</p><p>Some of the most memorable female solo artists from Norway are <a href="/wiki/Astrid_S" title="Astrid S">Astrid S</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adel%C3%A9n" title="Adel\xc3\xa9n">Adel\xc3\xa9n</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Bergan" title="Julie Bergan">Julie Bergan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maria_Mena" title="Maria Mena">Maria Mena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tone_Damli" title="Tone Damli">Tone Damli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Berger" title="Margaret Berger">Margaret Berger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lene_Marlin" title="Lene Marlin">Lene Marlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christel_Alsos" title="Christel Alsos">Christel Alsos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maria_Arredondo" title="Maria Arredondo">Maria Arredondo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lene_Nystr%C3%B8m" title="Lene Nystr\xc3\xb8m">Lene Nystr\xc3\xb8m</a> vocalist of the popular Danish dance band <a href="/wiki/Aqua_(band)" title="Aqua (band)">Aqua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marion_Raven" title="Marion Raven">Marion Raven</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Marit_Larsen" title="Marit Larsen">Marit Larsen</a> both former members of the defunct pop-rock band <a href="/wiki/M2M_(band)" title="M2M (band)">M2M</a>.\n</p><p>In recent years, various Norwegian songwriters and production teams have contributed to the music of other international artists. The Norwegian production team <a href="/wiki/Stargate_(production_team)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stargate (production team)">Stargate</a> has produced songs for <a href="/wiki/Rihanna" title="Rihanna">Rihanna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beyonc%C3%A9" title="Beyonc\xc3\xa9">Beyonc\xc3\xa9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shakira" title="Shakira">Shakira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie" title="Lionel Richie">Lionel Richie</a>, among others. <a href="/wiki/Espen_Lind" title="Espen Lind">Espen Lind</a> has written and produced songs for Beyonc\xc3\xa9, Lionel Richie and <a href="/wiki/Leona_Lewis" title="Leona Lewis">Leona Lewis</a>, among others. <a href="/wiki/Lene_Marlin" title="Lene Marlin">Lene Marlin</a> has written songs for Rihanna and <a href="/wiki/Lovebugs_(band)" title="Lovebugs (band)">Lovebugs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ina_Wroldsen" title="Ina Wroldsen">Ina Wroldsen</a> has written songs for artists such as <a href="/wiki/Demi_Lovato" title="Demi Lovato">Demi Lovato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shakira" title="Shakira">Shakira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inna" title="Inna">Inna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Ellis-Bextor" title="Sophie Ellis-Bextor">Sophie Ellis-Bextor</a>, <a href="/wiki/One_Direction" title="One Direction">One Direction</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Saturdays" title="The Saturdays">The Saturdays</a> among others.\n</p><p>Norway enjoys many music festivals throughout the year, all over the country. Norway is the host of one of the world\'s biggest <a href="/wiki/Extreme_sport" title="Extreme sport">extreme sport</a> festivals with music, <a href="/wiki/Ekstremsportveko" title="Ekstremsportveko">Ekstremsportveko</a>\xe2\x80\x94a festival held annually in <a href="/wiki/Voss" title="Voss">Voss</a>. Oslo is the host of many festivals, such as <a href="/wiki/%C3%98yafestivalen" title="\xc3\x98yafestivalen">\xc3\x98yafestivalen</a> and <a href="/wiki/By:Larm" title="By:Larm">by:Larm</a>. Oslo used to have a summer parade similar to the German <a href="/wiki/Love_Parade" title="Love Parade">Love Parade</a>. In 1992, the city of Oslo wanted to adopt the French music festival <i>F\xc3\xaate de la Musique</i>. <a href="/wiki/Fredrik_Carl_St%C3%B8rmer" title="Fredrik Carl St\xc3\xb8rmer">Fredrik Carl St\xc3\xb8rmer</a> established the festival. Even in its first year, "Musikkens Dag" gathered thousands of people and artists in the streets of Oslo. "Musikkens Dag" is now renamed <i>Musikkfest Oslo</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_literature" title="Norwegian literature">Norwegian literature</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Norwegian_writers" title="List of Norwegian writers">List of Norwegian writers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg/170px-Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg/255px-Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg/340px-Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2328" data-file-height="3300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamsun_bldsa_HA0269.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Knut_Hamsun" title="Knut Hamsun">Knut Hamsun</a>, author</div></div></div>\n<p>The history of Norwegian literature starts with the <a href="/wiki/Norse_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse paganism">pagan</a> <a href="/wiki/Poetic_Edda" title="Poetic Edda">Eddaic poems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skaldic</a> verse of the 9th and 10th centuries, with poets such as <a href="/wiki/Bragi_Boddason" title="Bragi Boddason">Bragi Boddason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eyvindr_sk%C3%A1ldaspillir" title="Eyvindr sk\xc3\xa1ldaspillir">Eyvindr sk\xc3\xa1ldaspillir</a>. The arrival of Christianity around the year 1000 brought Norway into contact with European mediaeval learning, <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> and history writing. Merged with native oral tradition and Icelandic influence, this influenced the literature written in the late 12th and early 13th centuries. Major works of that period include <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Norwegi%C3%A6" title="Historia Norwegi\xc3\xa6">Historia Norwegi\xc3\xa6</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%9Ei%C3%B0rekssaga" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x9ei\xc3\xb0rekssaga">\xc3\x9ei\xc3\xb0rekssaga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Konungs_skuggsj%C3%A1" title="Konungs skuggsj\xc3\xa1">Konungs skuggsj\xc3\xa1</a></i>.\n</p><p>Little Norwegian literature came out of the period of the Scandinavian Union and the subsequent Dano-Norwegian union (1387\xe2\x80\x931814), with some notable exceptions such as <a href="/wiki/Petter_Dass" title="Petter Dass">Petter Dass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Holberg" title="Ludvig Holberg">Ludvig Holberg</a>. In his play <i><a href="/wiki/Peer_Gynt" title="Peer Gynt">Peer Gynt</a></i>, Ibsen characterised this period as "Twice two hundred years of darkness/brooded o\'er the race of monkeys." The first line of this couplet is frequently quoted. During the union with Denmark, the government imposed using only written Danish, which decreased the writing of Norwegian literature.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibsen_photography.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ibsen_photography.jpg/170px-Ibsen_photography.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ibsen_photography.jpg/255px-Ibsen_photography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ibsen_photography.jpg/340px-Ibsen_photography.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1375" data-file-height="1979" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibsen_photography.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henrik_Ibsen" title="Henrik Ibsen">Henrik Ibsen</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Two major events precipitated a major resurgence in Norwegian literature: in 1811 a Norwegian university was established in <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Christiania</a>. Secondly, seized by the spirit of revolution following the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French</a> revolutions, the Norwegians created their first <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Constitution</a> in 1814. Strong authors were inspired who became recognised first in Scandinavia, and then worldwide; among them were <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Wergeland" title="Henrik Wergeland">Henrik Wergeland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Christen_Asbj%C3%B8rnsen" title="Peter Christen Asbj\xc3\xb8rnsen">Peter Christen Asbj\xc3\xb8rnsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgen_Moe" title="J\xc3\xb8rgen Moe">J\xc3\xb8rgen Moe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camilla_Collett" title="Camilla Collett">Camilla Collett</a>.\n</p><p>By the late 19th century, in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> of Norwegian literature, the so-called "Great Four" emerged: <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Ibsen" title="Henrik Ibsen">Henrik Ibsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson" title="Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson">Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kielland" title="Alexander Kielland">Alexander Kielland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Lie_(writer)" title="Jonas Lie (writer)">Jonas Lie</a>. Bj\xc3\xb8rnson\'s "peasant novels", such as <i>Ein glad gut</i> (A Happy Boy) and <i>Synn\xc3\xb8ve Solbakken</i>, are typical of the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_romantic_nationalism" title="Norwegian romantic nationalism">Norwegian romantic nationalism</a> of their day. Kielland\'s novels and short stories are mostly naturalistic. Although an important contributor to early romantic nationalism, (especially <i><a href="/wiki/Peer_Gynt" title="Peer Gynt">Peer Gynt</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Ibsen" title="Henrik Ibsen">Henrik Ibsen</a> is better known for his pioneering realistic dramas such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild_Duck" title="The Wild Duck">The Wild Duck</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Doll%27s_House" title="A Doll&#39;s House">A Doll\'s House</a>.</i> They caused an uproar because of his candid portrayals of the middle classes, complete with infidelity, unhappy marriages, and corrupt businessmen.\n</p><p>In the 20th century, three Norwegian novelists were awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>: <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson" title="Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson">Bj\xc3\xb8rnstjerne Bj\xc3\xb8rnson</a> in 1903, <a href="/wiki/Knut_Hamsun" title="Knut Hamsun">Knut Hamsun</a> for the book <i><a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Soil" title="Growth of the Soil">Markens gr\xc3\xb8de</a></i> ("Growth of the Soil") in 1920, and <a href="/wiki/Sigrid_Undset" title="Sigrid Undset">Sigrid Undset</a> (known for <i><a href="/wiki/Kristin_Lavransdatter" title="Kristin Lavransdatter">Kristinlavransdatter</a></i>) in 1928. Writers such as the following also made important contributions: <a href="/wiki/Dag_Solstad" title="Dag Solstad">Dag Solstad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Fosse" title="Jon Fosse">Jon Fosse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cora_Sandel" title="Cora Sandel">Cora Sandel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olav_Duun" title="Olav Duun">Olav Duun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olav_H._Hauge" title="Olav H. Hauge">Olav H. Hauge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunvor_Hofmo" title="Gunvor Hofmo">Gunvor Hofmo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stein_Mehren" title="Stein Mehren">Stein Mehren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kjell_Askildsen" title="Kjell Askildsen">Kjell Askildsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Herbj%C3%B8rnsrud" title="Hans Herbj\xc3\xb8rnsrud">Hans Herbj\xc3\xb8rnsrud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aksel_Sandemose" title="Aksel Sandemose">Aksel Sandemose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bergljot_Hob%C3%A6k_Haff" title="Bergljot Hob\xc3\xa6k Haff">Bergljot Hob\xc3\xa6k Haff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jostein_Gaarder" title="Jostein Gaarder">Jostein Gaarder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erik_Fosnes_Hansen" title="Erik Fosnes Hansen">Erik Fosnes Hansen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jens_Bj%C3%B8rneboe" title="Jens Bj\xc3\xb8rneboe">Jens Bj\xc3\xb8rneboe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kjartan_Fl%C3%B8gstad" title="Kjartan Fl\xc3\xb8gstad">Kjartan Fl\xc3\xb8gstad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lars_Saabye_Christensen" title="Lars Saabye Christensen">Lars Saabye Christensen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johan_Borgen" title="Johan Borgen">Johan Borgen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbj%C3%B8rg_Wassmo" title="Herbj\xc3\xb8rg Wassmo">Herbj\xc3\xb8rg Wassmo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Erik_Vold" title="Jan Erik Vold">Jan Erik Vold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolf_Jacobsen_(poet)" title="Rolf Jacobsen (poet)">Rolf Jacobsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Bull" title="Olaf Bull">Olaf Bull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Kj%C3%A6rstad" title="Jan Kj\xc3\xa6rstad">Jan Kj\xc3\xa6rstad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Johannesen" title="Georg Johannesen">Georg Johannesen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarjei_Vesaas" title="Tarjei Vesaas">Tarjei Vesaas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_Hoel" title="Sigurd Hoel">Sigurd Hoel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnulf_%C3%98verland" title="Arnulf \xc3\x98verland">Arnulf \xc3\x98verland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ove_Knausg%C3%A5rd" title="Karl Ove Knausg\xc3\xa5rd">Karl Ove Knausg\xc3\xa5rd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johan_Falkberget" title="Johan Falkberget">Johan Falkberget</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Internationally recognised Norwegian scientists include the mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Niels_Henrik_Abel" title="Niels Henrik Abel">Niels Henrik Abel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophus_Lie" title="Sophus Lie">Sophus Lie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atle_Selberg" title="Atle Selberg">Atle Selberg</a>, physical chemist <a href="/wiki/Lars_Onsager" title="Lars Onsager">Lars Onsager</a>, physicist <a href="/wiki/Ivar_Giaever" title="Ivar Giaever">Ivar Giaever</a>, chemists <a href="/wiki/Odd_Hassel" title="Odd Hassel">Odd Hassel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Waage" title="Peter Waage">Peter Waage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cato_Maximilian_Guldberg" title="Cato Maximilian Guldberg">Cato Maximilian Guldberg</a>.\n</p><p>In the 20th century, Norwegian academics have been pioneering in many <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Criminology" title="Criminology">criminology</a>, sociology and <a href="/wiki/Peace_and_conflict_studies" title="Peace and conflict studies">peace and conflict studies</a>. Prominent academics include <a href="/wiki/Arne_N%C3%A6ss" title="Arne N\xc3\xa6ss">Arne N\xc3\xa6ss</a>, a philosopher and founder of <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecology</a>; <a href="/wiki/Johan_Galtung" title="Johan Galtung">Johan Galtung</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Peace_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace studies">peace studies</a>; <a href="/wiki/Nils_Christie" title="Nils Christie">Nils Christie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mathiesen" title="Thomas Mathiesen">Thomas Mathiesen</a>, criminologists; <a href="/wiki/Fredrik_Barth" title="Fredrik Barth">Fredrik Barth</a>, a social anthropologist; <a href="/wiki/Vilhelm_Aubert" title="Vilhelm Aubert">Vilhelm Aubert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Holter" title="Harriet Holter">Harriet Holter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erik_Gr%C3%B8nseth" title="Erik Gr\xc3\xb8nseth">Erik Gr\xc3\xb8nseth</a>, sociologists; <a href="/wiki/Tove_Stang_Dahl" title="Tove Stang Dahl">Tove Stang Dahl</a>, a pioneer of women\'s law; <a href="/wiki/Stein_Rokkan" title="Stein Rokkan">Stein Rokkan</a>, a political scientist; and economists <a href="/wiki/Ragnar_Frisch" title="Ragnar Frisch">Ragnar Frisch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trygve_Haavelmo" title="Trygve Haavelmo">Trygve Haavelmo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Finn_E._Kydland" title="Finn E. Kydland">Finn E. Kydland</a>.\n</p><p>In 2014, the two Norwegian scientists <a href="/wiki/May-Britt_Moser" title="May-Britt Moser">May-Britt Moser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Moser" title="Edvard Moser">Edvard Moser</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> along with <a href="/wiki/John_O%27Keefe_(neuroscientist)" title="John O&#39;Keefe (neuroscientist)">John O\'Keefe</a>. They won the prize for their groundbreaking work identifying the cells that make up a positioning system in the human brain, our "in-built GPS".<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Norway" title="Architecture of Norway">Architecture of Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Urnesstavkirke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Urnesstavkirke.jpg/330px-Urnesstavkirke.jpg" width="330" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Urnesstavkirke.jpg/495px-Urnesstavkirke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Urnesstavkirke.jpg/660px-Urnesstavkirke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Urnesstavkirke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Urnes_Stave_Church" title="Urnes Stave Church">Urnes Stave Church</a> has been listed by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>With expansive forests, Norway has long had a tradition of building in wood. Many of today\'s most interesting new buildings are made of wood, reflecting the strong appeal that this material continues to hold for Norwegian designers and builders.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg/220px-Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg/330px-Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg/440px-Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1707" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalen_Hotell_IMG_4773.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dalen_Hotel" title="Dalen Hotel">Dalen Hotel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Telemark" title="Telemark">Telemark</a> built in <a href="/wiki/Dragestil" title="Dragestil">Dragon Style</a>, a style of design architecture that originated during the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_romantic_nationalism" title="Norwegian romantic nationalism">Norwegian romantic nationalism</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>With Norway\'s conversion to Christianity some 1,000 years ago, churches were built. Stonework architecture was introduced from Europe for the most important structures, beginning with the construction of <a href="/wiki/Nidaros_Cathedral" title="Nidaros Cathedral">Nidaros Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>. In the early <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, wooden <a href="/wiki/Stave_church" title="Stave church">stave churches</a> were constructed throughout Norway. Some of them have survived; they represent Norway\'s most unusual contribution to architectural history. A fine example, <a href="/wiki/Urnes_Stave_Church" title="Urnes Stave Church">Urnes Stave Church</a> in inner <a href="/wiki/Sognefjord" title="Sognefjord">Sognefjord</a>, is on <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_List" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage List">World Heritage List</a>. Another notable example of wooden architecture is the buildings at <a href="/wiki/Bryggen" title="Bryggen">Bryggen</a> Wharf in Bergen, also on the list for World Cultural Heritage sites, consisting of a row of tall, narrow wooden structures along the quayside.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hyttklokka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hyttklokka.jpg/220px-Hyttklokka.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hyttklokka.jpg/330px-Hyttklokka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hyttklokka.jpg/440px-Hyttklokka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="930" data-file-height="699" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hyttklokka.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 17th-century town of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8ros" title="R\xc3\xb8ros">R\xc3\xb8ros</a>, designated in 1980 as a UNESCO World Heritage Site, has narrow streets and wooden houses.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 17th century, under the Danish monarchy, cities and villages such as <a href="/wiki/Kongsberg" title="Kongsberg">Kongsberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8ros" title="R\xc3\xb8ros">R\xc3\xb8ros</a> were established. The city Kongsberg had a church built in the Baroque style. Traditional wooden buildings that were constructed in R\xc3\xb8ros have survived.\n</p><p>After Norway\'s union with Denmark was dissolved in 1814, Oslo became the capital. The architect <a href="/wiki/Christian_Heinrich_Grosch" title="Christian Heinrich Grosch">Christian H. Grosch</a> designed the earliest parts of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oslo" title="University of Oslo">University of Oslo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Stock_Exchange" title="Oslo Stock Exchange">Oslo Stock Exchange</a>, and many other buildings and churches constructed in that early national period.\n</p><p>At the beginning of the 20th century, the city of <a href="/wiki/%C3%85lesund" title="\xc3\x85lesund">\xc3\x85lesund</a> was rebuilt in the <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> style, influenced by styles of France. The 1930s, when functionalism dominated, became a strong period for Norwegian architecture. It is only since the late 20th century that Norwegian architects have achieved international renown. One of the most striking modern buildings in Norway is the <a href="/wiki/Sami_Parliament_of_Norway" title="Sami Parliament of Norway">Sami Parliament</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karasjok" title="Karasjok">K\xc3\xa1r\xc3\xa1\xc5\xa1johka</a>, designed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stein_Halvorson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stein Halvorson (page does not exist)">Stein Halvorson</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_Sundby&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christian Sundby (page does not exist)">Christian Sundby</a>. Its debating chamber, in timber, is an abstract version of a <i>lavvo,</i> the traditional tent used by the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_art" title="Norwegian art">Norwegian art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7783" data-file-height="5565" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adolph_Tidemand_%26_Hans_Gude_-_Bridal_Procession_on_the_Hardangerfjord_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Brudeferd i Hardanger</i> by <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Tidemand" title="Adolph Tidemand">Adolph Tidemand</a> og <a href="/wiki/Hans_Gude" title="Hans Gude">Hans Gude</a>, 1848</div></div></div>\n<p>For an extended period, the Norwegian art scene was dominated by artwork from Germany and Holland as well as by the influence of Copenhagen. It was in the 19th century that a truly Norwegian era began, first with portraits, later with impressive landscapes. Johan Christian Dahl (1788\xe2\x80\x931857), originally from the Dresden school, eventually returned to paint the landscapes of western Norway, defining Norwegian painting for the first time."<sup id="cite_ref-HFG_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HFG-274">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norway\'s newly found independence from Denmark encouraged painters to develop their Norwegian identity, especially with landscape painting by artists such as <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Lange_Kielland" title="Kitty Lange Kielland">Kitty Kielland</a>, a female painter who studied under <a href="/wiki/Hans_Gude" title="Hans Gude">Hans Gude</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Backer" title="Harriet Backer">Harriet Backer</a>, another pioneer among female artists, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">impressionism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frits_Thaulow" title="Frits Thaulow">Frits Thaulow</a>, an impressionist, was influenced by the art scene in Paris as was <a href="/wiki/Christian_Krohg" title="Christian Krohg">Christian Krohg</a>, a realist painter, famous for his paintings of prostitutes.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of particular note is <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Munch" title="Edvard Munch">Edvard Munch</a>, a symbolist/expressionist painter who became world-famous for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scream" title="The Scream">The Scream</a></i> which is said to represent the anxiety of modern man.\n</p><p>Other artists of note include <a href="/wiki/Harald_Sohlberg" title="Harald Sohlberg">Harald Sohlberg</a>, a neo-romantic painter remembered for his paintings of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8ros" title="R\xc3\xb8ros">R\xc3\xb8ros</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Odd_Nerdrum" title="Odd Nerdrum">Odd Nerdrum</a>, a figurative painter who maintains that his work is not art, but <a href="/wiki/Kitsch" title="Kitsch">kitsch</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_cuisine" title="Norwegian cuisine">Norwegian cuisine</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Category:Norwegian_cuisine" title="Category:Norwegian cuisine">Norway\'s culinary traditions</a> show the influence of long seafaring and farming traditions, with <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> (fresh and cured), <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> (pickled or marinated), <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">codfish</a>, and other seafood, balanced by cheeses (such as <a href="/wiki/Brunost" title="Brunost">brunost</a>), dairy products, and breads (predominantly dark/darker).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lefse" title="Lefse">Lefse</a> is a Norwegian potato flatbread, usually topped with large amounts of butter and sugar, most common around Christmas. Some traditional Norwegian dishes include <a href="/wiki/Lutefisk" title="Lutefisk">lutefisk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smalahove" title="Smalahove">smalahove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinnekj%C3%B8tt" title="Pinnekj\xc3\xb8tt">pinnekj\xc3\xb8tt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raspeball" title="Raspeball">raspeball</a>, and <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A5rik%C3%A5l" title="F\xc3\xa5rik\xc3\xa5l">f\xc3\xa5rik\xc3\xa5l</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Some quirky Norwegian speciality is rakefisk, which is a fermented trout, consumed with thin flatbread (flatbr\xc3\xb8d, not lefse) and sour cream. And the most popular pastry among all population is vaffel. It is different from Belgian in taste and consistency and is served with sour cream, brown cheese, butter and sugar, or strawberry or raspberry jam, which can all be mixed or eaten separately.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Football_in_Norway" title="Football in Norway">Football in Norway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg/220px-Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg/330px-Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg/440px-Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4896" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen_Holmenkollen_2011_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skier <a href="/wiki/Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen" title="Marit Bj\xc3\xb8rgen">Marit Bj\xc3\xb8rgen</a> from Norway is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_multiple_Winter_Olympic_medallists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of multiple Winter Olympic medallists">most successful Winter Olympian</a> of all time, with 15 medals</div></div></div>\n<p>Sports are a central part of Norwegian culture, and popular sports include association football, <a href="/wiki/Biathlon" title="Biathlon">biathlon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cross-country_skiing" title="Cross-country skiing">cross-country skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ski_jumping" title="Ski jumping">ski jumping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long_track_speed_skating" title="Long track speed skating">speed skating</a>, and, to a lesser degree, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Handball" title="Handball">handball</a>.\n</p><p>Association football is the most popular sport in Norway in terms of active membership. In 2014\xe2\x80\x932015 polling, football ranked far behind <a href="/wiki/Biathlon" title="Biathlon">biathlon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-country_skiing" title="Cross-country skiing">cross-country skiing</a> in terms of popularity as spectator sports.<sup id="cite_ref-Spopop_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spopop-277">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">Ice hockey</a> is the biggest indoor sport.<sup id="cite_ref-NorHall_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorHall-278">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_handball_team" title="Norway women&#39;s national handball team">women\'s handball national team</a> has won several titles, including two <a href="/wiki/Summer_Olympics" class="mw-redirect" title="Summer Olympics">Summer Olympics</a> championships (<a href="/wiki/Handball_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics" title="Handball at the 2008 Summer Olympics">2008</a>, <a href="/wiki/Handball_at_the_2012_Summer_Olympics" title="Handball at the 2012 Summer Olympics">2012</a>), three <a href="/wiki/World_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="World Women&#39;s Handball Championship">World Championships</a> (<a href="/wiki/1999_World_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="1999 World Women&#39;s Handball Championship">1999</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_World_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2011 World Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2011</a>, <a href="/wiki/2015_World_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2015 World Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2015</a>), and six <a href="/wiki/European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">European Championship</a> (<a href="/wiki/1998_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="1998 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">1998</a>, <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2004 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2004</a>, <a href="/wiki/2006_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2006 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2006</a>, <a href="/wiki/2008_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2008 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2008</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2010 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2010</a>, <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Women%27s_Handball_Championship" title="2014 European Women&#39;s Handball Championship">2014</a>).\n</p><p>In association football, the <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Norway women&#39;s national football team">Norwegian women\'s national team</a> has won the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="FIFA Women&#39;s World Cup">FIFA Women\'s World Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/1995_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="1995 FIFA Women&#39;s World Cup">1995</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_Olympics" class="mw-redirect" title="Football at the Olympics">Olympic Football Tournament</a> in <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2000 Summer Olympics \xe2\x80\x93 Women&#39;s tournament">2000</a>. The women\'s team also has two <a href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Women%27s_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA European Women&#39;s Championship">UEFA European Women\'s Championship</a> titles (<a href="/wiki/1987_European_Competition_for_Women%27s_Football" title="1987 European Competition for Women&#39;s Football">1987</a>, <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Women%27s_Euro_1993" title="UEFA Women&#39;s Euro 1993">1993</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Norway_national_football_team" title="Norway national football team">Norwegian men\'s national football team</a> has participated three times in the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> (<a href="/wiki/1938_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1938 FIFA World Cup">1938</a>, <a href="/wiki/1994_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1994 FIFA World Cup">1994</a>, <a href="/wiki/1998_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1998 FIFA World Cup">1998</a>), and once in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Football_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA European Football Championship">European Championship</a> (<a href="/wiki/UEFA_Euro_2000" title="UEFA Euro 2000">2000</a>). The highest FIFA ranking Norway has achieved is 2nd, a position it has held twice, in 1993 and in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-NorRank_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorRank-279">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a> is also gaining popularity in Norway. <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Carlsen" title="Magnus Carlsen">Magnus Carlsen</a> is the current world champion.<sup id="cite_ref-MagCham_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MagCham-280">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> There are about 10 <a href="/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess)" title="Grandmaster (chess)">Grandmasters</a> and 29 <a href="/wiki/International_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="International Masters">International Masters</a> in Norway.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg/240px-Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg" width="240" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg/360px-Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg/480px-Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holmenkollbakken_in_background.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ski jumping hill <a href="/wiki/Holmenkollbakken" title="Holmenkollbakken">Holmenkollbakken</a> in Oslo during the <a href="/wiki/FIS_Nordic_World_Ski_Championships_2011" title="FIS Nordic World Ski Championships 2011">FIS Nordic World Ski Championships 2011</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Bandy" title="Bandy">Bandy</a> is a traditional sport in Norway and the country is one of the four founders of <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_International_Bandy" title="Federation of International Bandy">Federation of International Bandy</a>. In terms of licensed athletes, it is the second biggest winter sport in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> As of January 2018, <a href="/wiki/Norway_national_bandy_team" title="Norway national bandy team">the men\'s national team</a> has captured one silver and one bronze, while <a href="/wiki/Norway_women%27s_national_bandy_team" title="Norway women&#39;s national bandy team">the women\'s national team</a> has managed five bronzes at <a href="/wiki/Bandy#World_Championships" title="Bandy">the World Championships</a>.\n</p><p>Norway first participated at the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> in 1900, and has sent athletes to compete in every Games since then, except for the sparsely attended <a href="/wiki/1904_Summer_Olympics" title="1904 Summer Olympics">1904 Games</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a> in Moscow when they participated in the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott" title="1980 Summer Olympics boycott">American-led boycott</a>. Norway leads <a href="/wiki/All-time_Olympic_Games_medal_table" title="All-time Olympic Games medal table">the overall medal tables</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Winter_Olympic_Games" title="Winter Olympic Games">Winter Olympic Games</a> with considerable margin. Famous Norwegian winter sport athletes includes biathlete <a href="/wiki/Ole_Einar_Bj%C3%B8rndalen" title="Ole Einar Bj\xc3\xb8rndalen">Ole Einar Bj\xc3\xb8rndalen</a>, speed skaters <a href="/wiki/Johan_Olav_Koss" class="mw-redirect" title="Johan Olav Koss">Johan Olav Koss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hjalmar_Andersen" title="Hjalmar Andersen">Hjalmar Andersen</a>, figure skater <a href="/wiki/Sonja_Henie" title="Sonja Henie">Sonja Henie</a> and cross country skiers <a href="/wiki/Marit_Bj%C3%B8rgen" title="Marit Bj\xc3\xb8rgen">Marit Bj\xc3\xb8rgen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rn_D%C3%A6hlie" title="Bj\xc3\xb8rn D\xc3\xa6hlie">Bj\xc3\xb8rn D\xc3\xa6hlie</a>.\n</p><p>Norway has hosted the Games on two occasions:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/1952_Winter_Olympics" title="1952 Winter Olympics">1952 Winter Olympics</a> in Oslo</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/1994_Winter_Olympics" title="1994 Winter Olympics">1994 Winter Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lillehammer" title="Lillehammer">Lillehammer</a></li></ul>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_rankings">International rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: International rankings">edit</a><span income.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="width:65%;">Index\n</th>\n<th style="width:15%;">Rank\n</th>\n<th style="width:20%;">Countries href="/wiki/Historical_capitals_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical capitals of Norway">Historical capitals of Norway</a></li></ul>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Norway&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_Treaty" title="Svalbard Treaty">Spitsbergen Treaty</a> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_Treaty" title="Svalbard Treaty">Svalbard Treaty</a>) of 9 February 1920 recognises Norway\'s full and absolute sovereignty over the arctic archipelago of Spitsbergen (now called <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Board1986_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Board1986-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_Treaty" title="Svalbard Treaty">Spitsbergen Treaty</a> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Svalbard_Treaty" title="Svalbard Treaty">Svalbard Treaty</a>) of 9 February 1920 recognises Norway\'s full and absolute sovereignty over the arctic archipelago of Spitsbergen (now called <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Board1986_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Board1986-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span
